Soul Hosts
Joseph Isaacs
Chapter 1 							
The Gray Man
	
I do not know what game the gray man plays -Kolram	

	-<>-

Forking right at the blacksmith's shop, Wayden kept a brisk pace. His worn leather shoes slapped the cobblestones, sweat drenched his much-patched robe. Glancing back through glaring sunlight, he spotted his black-hooded stalker, jostling through a crowd of market-goers, sword swaying at his side. 
Wayden's heartbeat thundered in his ears. The wizard in his mind calmed him, but it only helped so much.
"Why would someone be following a lowly orphan like me?"  Wayden asked the mage.
  Wayden's forehead itched, signaling that Grandmaster Kolram was preparing to respond. "'Tis strange. One wouldn't think a fifteen-year-old dressed in worn robes would make a likely target for a thief."
Wayden tried to stay calm, to think. "Maybe he's after the bounty on unauthorized use of magic?" 
"But how could he be aware of my presence?" Kolram asked.
"I haven't told anyone."
Wayden risked a glance. His pursuer's face . . . gray . . . an Ozac. Wayden's shoulders tightened. Could he be the same one? 
No. 
This one was only six feet tall- a foot shorter than the one who killed Nanny.
A familiar face, a friendly acolyte who helped run services at the red temple, headed towards an alley. 
"Thank the Source! He'll help me." 
"Wait!" Kolram said.
 Wayden darted in front of a rickshaw and past a man selling fish at a stand.
"Now we are trapped in a deserted alley with no one to help us but a weak acolyte," Kolram grumbled.
Wayden wavered at the alley entrance. Should he follow the acolyte deeper into the alley or try to dash past the Ozac?
Across the street, the stalker glared, red eyes burning beneath his cowl. Wayden's stomach churned. The Ozac stepped forward, but a merchant stepped in his path. "Fish, for you, Sir. Very good quality."
"No." He tried to step around the merchant, but the fishmonger moved with him as if they were disgruntled dance partners.
"How much you want to pay? I'll give you a deal."
Wayden barely registered the words. His pursuer pulled his hood back, revealing silver hair and gray skin mottled with green blemishes. Memories of the first Ozac Wayden had ever seen flashed through his mind - the muscled behemoth had shattered the barricaded door with his spiked mace.
"Focus, Wayden," Kolram said. "You need to be in the moment."
Wayden breathed deeply, slowing his hammering heart. This wasn't the same dark-gray giant from his nightmares. This one's skin was lighter and body leaner. He wielded a sword rather than a mace, but tall or short, mace or sword, being pursued by an Ozac was not a matter to be taken lightly. 
Wayden couldn't retreat now, the gray man blocked his path. He bolted into the alley, the fishmonger's voice fading behind him, "Ah, you're an Ozac. I do a lot of business in Alatia."
The tail of the acolyte's maroon cloak disappeared around a corner into an adjoining alley. Wayden decided the acolyte was his best bet. He followed, making his way past stacked kegs and timber. 
The alley walls seemed to close in. A noxious odor assaulted him. A trickle of foul water flowed through the cracked ground beneath his feet. 
Wayden followed the acolyte into yet another alley. "It's a maze back here."
"Tis strangely elaborate," Kolram said.
Wayden turned another corner, hoping to find salvation, and instead found himself facing a wall of muscle. A broad-shouldered man towered over him. A tattoo of a viper spiraled across his features, its inked tail running across the expanse of his bare chest. The hulk of a man seized Wayden's robed arm. 
"Where you thinkin' you be goin'?"
The man's breath was nauseating - an odd mix of tobacco, liver, and booze.
"Oh! Sorry, wrong turn." Wayden tried to pull away, but the man's grip tightened. 
Behind Viper-Tattoo, Wayden saw the acolyte handing a coin to a short man with a huge mole on his nose. Mole-nose pocketed the coin and began to trace circles in the air. He flexed his fingers and chanted in a deep guttural voice. A mist emerged from the acolyte's mouth, slowly rising.
The acolyte's face grew pale and his eyes bulged, veins protruded from his sweat-slicked forehead. Wayden wondered if he was witnessing a murder, until Mole-nose suddenly ceased his chanting and the acolyte's face returned to normal. 
The acolyte murmured, "More. Please, more." The mist slipped back into the acolyte's mouth. "No. . . don't stop . . . please."
"A spell used as a narcotic?" Kolram mused.
 "No time for that now," Wayden responded. "I've got to escape!"
Viper-Tattoo's grip remained solid.
"Boss, look what I be findin'," the big man said to Mole-nose.
The acolyte's eyes widened with recognition as he caught Wayden's gaze. He did not look nearly as friendly as he had in the temple. He swore with words that caused Wayden to blush, and then rushed down a side passage, leaving Wayden alone with Mole-nose and his goon. 
Wayden swallowed hard.
Mole-nose sauntered forward, his eyes narrowed. He wore a tan robe filigreed with silver swirls - well dressed for a man who hung around alleys. His voice was as sharp as the curved dagger hanging on his belt. "Who sent you here, boy?"
"No one."
Mole-nose drew the blade. "Just like exploring back alleyways, do you?"
Wayden closed his eyes. This is it. Mother, I'm coming to join you.
 "Ah, son, there you are!" His pursuer emerged from around the corner. "What are you doing back here?" 
Wayden's insides twisted. The question now was not whether he'd be caught, but which beast would do the catching. 
"Please pardon my son." The gray man smiled at Mole-nose. "He's a bit short on brains. Can't leave the boy for a moment without him wandering off."
Viper-Tattoo laughed. "Your father be an Ozac? No wonder you be so dumb."
Mole-nose snorted. "You're telling me that's your son? You take me for a fool?"
"I take you for a man to be honored and respected." The Ozac removed a jingling bag of coins from his robe. He took out a silver piece. "I'm his stepfather. I am most sorry to have troubled you. Please take this gift as a token of my sincere regret. Come along, son. We're going to be late." 
Mole-nose nodded at Viper-Tattoo, who released Wayden's arm. A gray skinned hand replaced it. Wayden had never asked to host Kolram. Now he'd go to prison for it. It was unfair.
Wayden tried to pull free again, as the Ozac led him onto the main street. "Let. Me. Go!"
 "Boy, let me explain-" the Ozac began.
"Please, Sir." The fishmonger grabbed at the Ozac's arm and shook it. "Business is slow and my child is very sick." 
Wayden saw his opportunity. He wrenched his arm free and bolted.
 "Wait!" the Ozac shouted.
Waiting was the last thing on Wayden's mind. Weaving through the throng of merchants and buyers, he almost tripped over a beggar child shaking a tin can. A quick glance revealed no pursuer. After running like this for a notch of the sun dial, he slowed, not wanting to be overconfident of escape, but also wary of attracting attention from the city guard.
Another notch later, he reached the orphanage gate and allowed himself a deep breath. The sign, hung from an iron fence read, 'The Dracon's Home for Unadoptable Boys', but today it might as well have read 'Safety'.
Never thought I'd be happy to see that sign.
Rory, a burly orphanage worker, leaned against the gate bars. He stared at Wayden with his good eye, the lazy one staring off into space. "Do you have it, Wayden?" 
"Quick." Wayden's shoulders tightened. "Open. I'll give it to you once I'm inside." 
"What's your rush?" Rory asked scratching behind one of his elephantine ears. "The wraiths after you?"
"Rory, please."
 Rory pulled up a bolt and swung the gate open. "Did she give it to you?"
With the clang of metal closing behind him, Wayden's heart settled. He searched his pocket, afraid the letter had been lost, but it was still there. The parchment, and Wayden's robes, were damp with perspiration, but it was still legible. 
"Go on then." Rory nodded at the letter. "Read it."
Wayden cleared his throat. "Me dearest honeysuckles, you be as beautiful as a dragon's egg. I be lovin' you something fierce. Love and kisses, Sugar Pie."
Rory smiled. "That's lovely." He took the note from Wayden and tenderly studied it before placing it into his pocket. His face hardened and he glared at Wayden. "Don't tell anyone about this, and come by later to get me response. Don't forget, or I'll be makin' you sorry, Toast."
Wayden's burn-marked forehead tightened at Rory reverting to his nickname. He was Wayden when Rory wanted something, but as soon as the favor was done, it was back to Toast.
Wayden turned and looked toward the street, half fearing to see his pursuer standing there, but he saw only market goers and merchants. Was that the last Wayden would see of the Ozac? If he was followed because of Kolram, how had the gray man known?
 "Do you think the Ozac might be a Magic-finder?" Wayden asked.
"Magic-finders' hands glow when they draw near practitioners of magic," Kolram said. "You would know this if you paid better attention to my lectures."
"What's that, Kolram? I fell asleep. You were lecturing."
"If only I had been hosted by your brother."
The wizard's remarks irked him. They echoed his mother's endless unfavorable comparisons to his twin-brother, Mavik. Why can't you study like your brother? Well, Mavik had studied. Had it done him any good?
A voice from the crowded street caught Wayden's attention. That voice- could it be? The sensation of ice snakes slithered down his spine. "Excuse me. Coming through."
The silver-haired Ozac was pushing past a beggar woman and making his way to the gate. Wayden froze unsure whether to run, hide, cry, or scream. He settled for standing there looking stupid.
"I need to talk to your master," the gray man said to Rory.
No! So unfair! I was sure I'd escaped.
A soldier rode around the bend. If Wayden's heart had been sinking at the sight of the Ozac, it now had completely submerged. The Ozac could just call to the soldier and have them seize Wayden, but the gray man watched the guard ride away wordlessly. Wayden exhaled.
"Hmm," Kolram said, "Perhaps he isn't trying to collect a bounty after all."
"Then why is he following me?"
"I do not know what game the gray man plays."
The orphans, most with red hair like Wayden, stopped weeding the vegetable path and gawked at the gangly Ozac.
 "You can speak with me," Rory said. "I'm second-in-command."
"I'm sure you are a man of great skill," the Ozac said. "Unfortunately, it is to the master I must speak. Please take this as a token of my appreciation." The Ozac pulled out a coin from his purse. Rory's eyes widened as the Ozac passed the money through the gate's bars.
Rory pocketed it with a smile, pushed the squeaky rod up, and swung the gate open. "Why, come right in, Sir."
Wayden's legs quivered as he watched the Ozac stride forward. His cloak swung open, revealing a black doublet embroidered with golden coils. He leaned close to Wayden. "Don't look so terrified, boy. If I wanted you dead, you'd be on the ash pyre by now." He smiled, revealing pointed teeth.
Was the Ozac telling the truth? He hadn't done anything to him. But then why was he following him?
The sound of hoof beats and voices from the road drew Wayden's attention. "Make way for the Guard Draconi! In the name of the Dracon, stand aside!" A second group of soldiers on a horse-drawn wagon were shouting at the crowd. To Wayden's alarm, they were heading straight towards the orphanage. 
"Open the gates!" one of the orange-caped guards commanded.
One of the guards guided the wagon to a halt in the yard, while a second soldier climbed out of the rear and appeared to be helping someone down. The Ozac's eyes widened and glimmered, staring at this new arrival. And then the guard shifted and Wayden caught sight of the new arrival was a boy about his own age . . . purple hair . . . dark skin . . . a Tulkarian.
Wayden's heart beat like thunder. He clenched his teeth.
 First the Ozac, and now this. Two ghosts from the past in one day. Memories of that horrendous day flooded Wayden's mind. A tall Tulkarian Sky Raider, purple hair pulled back in a ponytail . . . his big black-winged wolf  by his side . . . black-feathered arrow nocked and readied . . . mother's face . . . the cruel smile . . .a flower, a flower . . . red flower.
But just as it hadn't been the same Ozac, this was not the same Tulkarian. This one was short, thin, and vulnerable. He was the perfect target for the rage that had been building up inside Wayden all these years. It was not the Tulkarian or the Ozac that truly scared him, but the anger inside himself, boiling, ready to erupt.





Chapter 2 					
Cold-Blooded Killers Wanted

If she lifts up a chamber pot, you'd best duck. - Cook
-<>-
For the first time in seven years, Wayden was in the presence of both a Tulkarian and an Ozac. He found his memory jerked back to that dismal day seven years ago . . . the day his world fell apart. He had been short for an eight-year old, and so he clambered onto the inside ledge of his manor's bay window. The invasion horn blared again - three short trills followed by a long one. The approaching sky wolves, at first just dots, sprouted wings as they drew closer. Excitement swirled in Wayden's mind. He'd see a real battle!
Mavik, Nanny, and Mother erected a rickety barricade in front of the door using every piece of furniture available from the foyer. Then they waited. The stomping of boots and men's rough voices signaled an enemy's approach. A rank smell filled the air.
The door shuddered as something slammed into it. A second blow split the door into jagged splinters. The remnants of the door and the barricade gave way, as the Ozac crashed through. He was followed by two others wearing the black studded armor of Skyraiders and necklaces of dried fingers. A one-eared Sky-Raider touched a torch to the drapes. Flames consumed the fabric.
 The Ozac swung his mace, crushing Nanny's jaw, sending her shattered teeth skittering across the floor. Her body crumpled to the floor, a halo of blood forming around her disfigured head. 
Wayden felt a shriek boil up inside him. It seemed to course through his whole body. What happened next was a blur. Somehow he ended up running while holding both his mother's hand and Mavik's. All three of them were in tears as they raced through the blazing house. A torch had been thrown through the broken scullery window, and the thickening smoke forced them into a slow crawl. The ceiling groaned. 
Wayden looked up just as a burning cinder plummeted onto him. Grabbing his injured forehead, he screamed, releasing his brother's hand. By the time he caught another glimpse of Mavik, a man with a goat's face was pulling him out the back door. 
His mother chased the goat man, towing Wayden along with her. They raced out onto the rear porch. A Tulkarian Sky Raider waited in the yard, arrow nocked. His long purple hair swayed as he released the bow string. 
Mother's grip tightened. She turned, revealing a bloody arrow shaft buried in her chest. It was just like the watercolor prophecy his brother Mavik had painted the day before, of the crimson flower forming on her lily-white dress-
Wayden's world was his mother. The sounds of battle barely touched the periphery of his attention. His eyes couldn't leave her, her head in his lap, pale-faced, tears moistening her cheeks, lips trembling. 
 "I'm sorry, Wayden." Her voice was so weak, so faint.
"No. You can't die." The ground felt like it was crumbling beneath him. He remembered thinking he never got to eat his apple crumble. He flushed with shame at the memory.
Wayden never could recall what happened next. From what he could piece together afterwards, Red Kingdom soldiers arrived with several dragons and chased off the invaders. His next clear memory was of a bumpy wagon ride to Vilanos crowded in with other refugees from his decimated hometown.
Wayden blinked away the memory. The pain was washed away by the drug of anger, as he stared at the trembling purple-haired boy being escorted through the muddy yard. The seashells in the boy's braids clicked with each step.
 "A Tulkarian." Wayden gritted his teeth. "A wraithin' Tulkarian." 
 "Language, young Wayden," Grandmaster Kolram said. "No need to use the 'w' word. This is not the Tulkarian Sky Raider who killed your mother. That one was two feet taller, a decade older, and wore a necklace of dried fingers, as I recall."
"A Tulkarian is a Tulkarian."
"And a Helesian is a Helesian," Kolram responded. "The Sky Raider who torched your mansion had the red hair so common in our land. Should all red-haired Helesians be accountable for burning down your house?"
The guards flanked the Tulkarian boy, who shuffled forward, lifting his bloodshot, swollen eyes from the ground only for moments at a time. Tears streaked his puffy cheeks and his lip trembled. 
A strange sensation pulled at Wayden, growing in intensity as the boy approached. It was an odd sensation. It was as if an invisible string connected him to the Tulkarian. The boy might have felt it too, for the few times he did glance upwards, he looked at Wayden curiously. 
Wayden had felt a bond like this towards Mavik. He'd thought it was because they were twins, but if so, why would he be linked with a Tulkarian? His stomach twisted and churned.
The guard escorting the Tulkarian nodded at Rory. The orange cape and the single flame on his surcoat marked him as a low-ranking Flicker.
"You brought us some fresh meat," Rory said, with a grin. 
"It's been a long day for us, Rory," the guard said. "We had to haul this one's mother over to the Volcano Palace for unauthorized use of magic. On top of that, we found another murdered girl."
"The Striker again?" Rory asked.
"The dead girl was red-headed, young, pretty, no marks on her body. Sounds like the Striker to me."
"You need to get him behind bars, or better yet, in a grave." Rory fingered his folded love letter. "It ain't safe for a lady no more." 
"Them lot couldn't catch a fish from a bowl of Cook's stew," a voice boomed from the doorway. Master Crag's beady eyes gleamed at the new orphan. "Brought us a Tulkarian, eh? Well, we always do get the bottom of the barrel here. Still, the Draconess is coming soon to do her count, so another head means another coin."
Crag stepped off the front stoop, the heels of his ox-hide boots squishing in the muddy yard. He wore a new hat, this one with only three brims and spiky orange feathers on top.
The guard muttered to himself as he headed back to the wagon. The Ozac stood there watching everything, a big grin on his face. 
"What's your name, boy?" Crag asked the Tulkarian.
"I'm R-R-Rif."
Stupid Tulkarian can't even talk right, Wayden thought.
"Well, R-R-Rif," Crag said. "You'd be wise not to cause any trouble." He motioned with his cane at Wayden. "Toast, show the new boy around. Oh and get my grandmother her stew. That'll be your job from now on."
Wayden felt like he'd just taken two kicks to the stomach. Showing the Tulkarian around was bad enough, but Crag's grandmother? She was a known loon.
"Me? But I thought the new maid-"
"The new maid quit last night. My grandma decided to empty the contents of her chamber pot on her. Now it's your job."
"What? That's not-" Wayden blurted out.
"No arguing, Toast!" Crag said. He slapped a tarnished key into Wayden's palm. Crag turned to Rory, gesturing towards the Ozac. "Is this your new lady friend?"
Rory blushed. "Master Crag. If you please, this gentleman be wanting to have words with you."
"And who might you be?" Crag asked with a sneer.
"I'm called Anaz," the Ozac answered. "I'd like to discuss a private matter."
"You can like it all you want, you ain't getting it," Crag said, picking his teeth.
Anaz pulled out another of his coins. "I'll compensate you for your time." He handed the money to Crag.
 "Well, then," Crag smiled, holding the coin up to the light. "Follow me. What a lucky day this has been."
Lots of luck. All of it bad.
 Wayden chewed his nails as he watched Crag and Anaz disappear up the dimly lit wooden staircase that led to Crag's room. Rory made to follow, but Crag shook his head. "Go see how the Dariuses are getting along with gathering the rushes." 
Rory turned to Wayden. "What are you just standing around for? Aren't you supposed to be doing something?" 
Wayden yanked Rif's arm, towing him towards the kitchen. "If I need to show you around, then let's go."
Rif shuffled along behind him.
 "What do you think this Anaz is up to?" Wayden asked Kolram.
"I do not know," Kolram said. "If he was going to attack us or turn us to the Guard, he'd have done it by now, so I think we can rule that out. Perhaps he is here to get us to use my powers in some way."
 "I still don't get how he could know about you or that I know magic," Wayden mused. "Wouldn't he think I'm too young? There aren't any other magi my age, right?"
"None that I know of, apart from your brother," Kolram responded. "Perhaps if you hurry, you can listen in to what he is saying with Crag. Fetch the stew quickly from Cook, and you'll have an excuse to pass their door." 
Rif tugged at Wayden's sleeve. Wayden turned angrily, half hoping Rif would take a swing at him. Instead, the Tulkarian said, "I'm s-s-sorry if I've caused you any trouble."
Wayden snorted.
Stupid stuttering Tulkarian. Tulkarians, Ozacs, who would come next to the orphanage? A wraith? Perhaps they ought to hang a sign on the orphanage: 'Cold-blooded killers wanted.' Maybe they could get the Striker to stay as well.
"Wayden, some self-control!" Kolram said. "Some Tulkarians are killers, and some aren't. The same with every other race. Wherever the borders fall, people are just people."
There was no doorknob on the scullery, so Wayden stuck his hand into the hole where one ought to have been. A pair of metal tongs squeezed Wayden's fingers. Wayden shook his fingers in pain, as Cook cackled. 
"No one likes Cook's jokes except Cook," Kolram said, "but he likes them enough for everyone."
Cook was a man with a thousand warts, hairs growing out of each one. His ears were as thickly forested as his head. "Toast! And who's this?"
"A new boy - Rif," Wayden said, examining his hand. The tongs had left a red welt.
A tangy smell wafted through the air as they entered the kitchen. Wayden's hand still stung, and his anger simmered like the cauldron of stew on the hearth. Two of the orphans were cleaning dishes. The clanging metal sounded like a battle. 
Leaning over a cast-iron cauldron of stew, Cook spooned out a portion and offered it to Rif. "Try this." 
The brownish-gray stew glistened, a coat of oil blanketing its surface. Rif stared at it, lip curled, eyes wide, as if wary a tentacle might break from its surface and pull him in. Wayden's stomach, on the other hand, rumbled. He'd only been given a burnt piece of toast for breakfast, and a small portion of oats for lunch. Even Rif's seashell braided-hair looked delicious to Wayden at the moment. And the tray might be worth a bit of gnawing as well.
 "I'm not h-h-hungry."
"It's going to go into one of your holes, so you pick which." Cook sniggered.
Rif tipped the bread bowl, drinking from it a small sip. He gagged and spat out a strand of Cook's hair. Wayden never understood how Cook could have hair left on his temples when so much of it ended up in the stew. 
Cook slapped his thighs, his warts jiggling with his belly as he laughed. 
"Master Crag ordered us to bring food for his grandma," Wayden said.
 "Did he now? Ye'll be wearing the food, most like." Cook burst into another round of guffaws.
 "He should charge himself admission to his own show," Kolram suggested.
"Master Crag asked us to deliver the food quickly," Wayden added.
Cook gestured to one of the orphans doing the washing. "Tall Darius - get me Healer Berik's powder. We gotta give Mistress Night her calming medicine before she throws another chamber pot, don't we?"
The orphan hurried to take a vial of yellow powder out of a cabinet. Cook sprinkled it in a bread bowl of stew. He placed it on a wooden tray and handed it to Rif. 
"We put Mistress Night's medicine in there to help her sleep. She's a bit moody. If she lifts up her chamber pot, you'd best duck." Cook's laughter followed them as they headed out of the scullery into the main room.
One of the orphans paused from mopping, and waved at Rif. "Aye there Tulkarian. I be Handsome Darius. And this here is Little Darius." He gestured to a thin orphan, who was scrubbing the table. 
Before Rif could respond, Wayden said, "Come on, Tulkarian. Crag told us to hurry." Hopefully, he wouldn't be too late to overhear Crag's conversation with Anaz. 
As they headed up the stairs, Rif asked, "Why are so many people round here called D-D-Darius?"
"They're named in honor of the Immortal who founded Vilanos, Darius Dragonking."
"I thought he f-f-founded the whole Red Lands of Helos." 
"He did, but he lived here. Be quiet for a moment, I want to see if I can find out about this Ozac."
Rif looked puzzled at this, but said nothing as Wayden pressed his ear up against Crag's door. 
"I can't afford another guard," Crag said.
Anaz's voice had taken on the cordial tone he'd used with Mole-nose. "You don't need to pay me."
"You'll work here without pay? Why?" 
"I have my reasons. They won't matter to you," Anaz said.
"I see. Not asking questions is harder than you might think." 
"You mean more expensive than I thought." 
Wayden heard the jingling of a purse. That sounded like a fair amount of coin to give away. He was paying to work at the orphanage. He was obviously interested in Wayden and Rif, but why?
 "Well that ought to buy us brandy for the winter. We've a deal!"
Wayden raced away from the door towards Rif as footsteps approached. The door swung open, Crag emerged and stood on the landing, his arm around the Ozac's shoulder.
"Toast! Rif! Good, delivering the stew for my grandmother. Nice to see you being prompt for a change," Crag said, beaming. "Say hello to our new guard, Anaz." 
Wayden's tongue wouldn't operate, his lungs wouldn't fill. Luckily, after an awkward moment, Rif spoke for them, "Congratulations, M-m-master Anaz."
Crag stared into his coin purse, admiring his windfall. "Let's go to the inn for a drink to celebrate. Perhaps, I'll buy a new robe while we're out. I might be a Fire-Whisperer soon." Crag straightened his hat. "I've been selected in the choosing this year. I could be working for the Dracon at the Red Palace."
"What an honor that would be," Anaz said.
Crag and Anaz headed downstairs. Wayden shook his head. The man who had pursued him just a notch of the sundial ago was now under his roof. Why was he so interested in Wayden that he'd take a job for no pay?
 "H-h-hear anything interesting?" Rif asked.
For a brief moment Wayden considered confiding in the Tulkarian. Then he remembered he hated Tulkarians.
"Not really. Come on then."
"Th-th-thanks for showing me around."
The Tulkarian acted polite, but who knew what really lay beneath the surface?
"Exactly," Kolram said, "It could be good or bad, you don't know. Give the boy a chance."
"Has anyone ever given me a chance? He could be . . . " An thought chilled Wayden. "He could be the Striker."
"Hardly seems likely. What about Mole-nose? The Striker leaves victims without a mark on their body. That mist spell Mole-nose cast - was something about it-"
"What's your name?" Rif asked, as they proceeded up the staircase, past the storerooms on the fourth floor, towards the fifth and final floor. "It's not really T-T-Toast?"
 "No. My name isn't Toast. I'm Wayden." 
 "I had a friend in Tulkaria named Wayden. Why is the orphanage so much t-t-taller than the other buildings in Vilanos?"
"It used to be a tower for the insane," Wayden said. Then he added with a smile. "Still is, some say."
Then Wayden remembered he was supposed to hate this boy. He sped faster up the stairs, Rif panting after him. When they reached the fifth-floor landing, Wayden allowed himself a moment to take in his favorite view. The last rays of sunset shone sharp reds and rich purples on the horizon. The night was coming, and Wayden would spend it with an Ozac and a Tulkarian under his roof.
 A little shove and there would be no more Tulkarian.
"And who would be the cold-blooded killer then?" Kolram asked.
Wayden sighed. He knew the wizard was right. It was just that there was so much anger bubbling inside him.
"Save your rage for Gar Skymaster," Kolram thought. "It was he that ordered the raid. It is he who should face justice."
 "Is it true a dr-dr-dragon lives in there?" Rif pointed at the Red Palace. The volcanic fortress jutted upwards on the eastern skyline, its craterous tip like a dragon's mouth, and its sloped sides, folded wings. 
"It's true."
"I've never seen one." 
 "When did you move to Helos?"
"Just a month ago," Rif replied. 
"If you'd grown up here, you'd have seen hundreds of them, before the dragon plague. Even a year ago, you would have seen Volkanus, the last dragon, flying around. He's been sick lately, and there hasn't been a hatching in a while. There might be no more dragons soon."
Rif's eyes lit up and he pointed towards the three domed temples in the distance. "Ah, the Mercy is that w-w-way. That's where Ma and I lived."
 "So you lived near the Wall of Flames?" Wayden asked. "What's it like?"
 Far past the temple stood the Wall of Flames, where fiery plumes of lava burst up as far as the eye could see. Wayden dreamt he'd see the Wall for himself someday.
"The Wall was str-str-strange. No heat came from it, but they say if you touched it you would burn. How l-l-long have you been in the orphanage?" 
"Seven years ago, a Tulkarian loosed an arrow into my mother, ransacked my town, and kidnapped by brother. Been here ever since."
"I'm s-s-sorry."
Wayden spat on the stairs. "Aye, well, so was she."
"I hate T-T-Tulkar."
This caught Wayden off guard. "Why?" 
"Well, what the Diamond Kings have done to it, anyway. They've restarted the slave trade. My ma and I, we r-r-ran away. But my sisters and my father were enslaved," Rif wiped something from his eye. "What about your father? Was he killed by Tulkarians too?"
 "My father disappeared a decade ago, leading a quest to bring Gar Skymaster to justice," Wayden paused for a moment. Rif looked devastated at this news. Kolram might be right. Perhaps, this boy wasn't to blame. "When I turn sixteen, I'm going to become a free-sword and rescue my father and brother, if they're still alive. I'll see the Skymaster pay."
"There are a lot of the Sky R-R-Raiders, aren't there? And they have winged wolves. Are you sure you can fight all of them?"
"I'm sure," Wayden said. He knew it was foolish to think he could take on the Sky Raiders singlehandedly, but he'd never admit that aloud. Especially to a Tulkarian. At the same time he couldn't give up the hope of it. It was all that kept him going. 
Rif didn't seem bad, Wayden had to admit. The Ozac seemed like the greater threat. What could Wayden do about it though? Talking to the authorities was out. Even if they believed Wayden had never cast a spell in his life, it might not matter. Kolram suspected Dracon Niar might be collecting the magi for some nefarious purpose, and the unauthorized magic ban was just an excuse for it.
 One of Kolram's memories was of the Dracon summoning all the Grandmasters to a temple the day they were murdered. The Dracon ordered them to wear blindfolds to 'increase their concentration' for an important ritual. After that, the memories all went dark.
Rory was yelling something downstairs. How much time had Wayden wasted gawking at the scenery? The last rays of the sunset were gone, and the sky was a canopy of stars.
"Rory will be up here with his whip if we don't get moving," Wayden said, removing the keys from his pocket and fumbling to find the lock. In all his years in the orphanage, he'd never been inside her room. With a click, the door opened and they entered.
The stench from Mistress Night's chamber pot made Wayden crinkle his nose. On a small table, a yellowed parchment fluttered, its corner weighed down by an ink pot. 
 A large cauldron of water was mounted on a brazier in the corner of the room. Wayden's brother, Mavik, had owned a similar one, emblazoned with an eye, the symbol of the Splashers. Was Crag's grandmother a water prophetess? More importantly, where was she?
 A cross breeze tugged at Wayden. A trio of candle flames danced in the cool breeze that crept through billowing curtains. He stared at the open window.
 Then he heard it. An old woman's cackle. 
Coming from the window ledge. 
Chapter 3							
Stars and Rats

If you want to be a hero, be one. There is no secret to it. - Kolram

-<>-

Wayden rushed past Rif to the window. Mistress Night stood on the ledge, dressed in a white bed-gown, staring through a telescope. The wind whipped at her long, ghost-white hair, and her gown flapped against her legs. Twilight had faded, and Night was studying the heavens with her telescope. A shooting star flew past. Turning to look at it, she teetered on the narrow ledge. Wayden swallowed. If Crag's grandmother fell five stories on his first day on the job, he'd be accused of murder. 
"Mistress!" Wayden pleaded. "Please come back inside!"
Night turned and cackled. Her face was a crosshatch of scars. "Why don't you come get me?"
 "Please, Mistress. You'll fall."
"Come out here with me, or else . . ." Mistress Night said, dangling one foot over the edge and waggling it.
"If I come out there, will you come back in with me?" 
She nodded, the wind blowing her hair like a flag of surrender.
Wayden took a breath and pulled himself onto the ledge. His knees wobbled as he inched sideways. He could see the street-dwellers' tiny fires flickering below, as well as the illuminated windows of shops and homes. He didn't like to imagine what his body would look like after a five-story fall. Cool air blew past him, yet sweat still trickled down his forehead, stinging his eyes. The ground swirled beneath him. 
Night spun towards Wayden, moving her face within a hairsbreadth of his own. By some miracle he kept his balance. Her breath carried the smell of dead fish, as she leaned in and kissed Wayden on the lips. He tasted cod liver oil. His stomach heaved. 
When skin touched skin, Kolram's Glimpser powers took Wayden. His thoughts swirled as he sank into Night's mind. 
Wayden was a young Night now. Night clutched her dolly as she watched snow pile against the frosted windows. She was waiting in the cabin for Mother to return -half afraid she wouldn't, and half afraid she would.
She eyed the trapdoor Mother had forbidden her to enter. Something dangerous lived down there. Night took the key from the hollow in the statuette where Mother had hidden it. With a click, she unlocked the hatch, lifting it to reveal a long dark tunnel. 
Just as quickly as they'd come, Night's memories faded. Wayden pulled away, spluttering, unsure if he was in a cabin in the snow or on a ledge. Looking down, he could see it was the latter. He tottered, but managed to grab the ridge between the wooden sideboards keeping his balance.
Night snickered. "You didn't like my kiss?"
Wayden couldn't speak. Couldn't breathe.
"Mistress Night," Rif called from the window. "You promised you would come b-b-b-back in."
"A promise made is a promise kept." Night slapped the telescope against Wayden's chest and clambered back through the window. Rif helped Night down from the ledge. Wayden, still trembling, climbed back in. He leaned for a moment against the window glass giving a silent prayer of thanks to Darius for saving him.
"Let us celebrate!" Night threw her arms into the air and spun, her gown billowing out like an upside down lily.
"C-c-celebrate what?" Rif asked, looking as pale as Wayden felt.
"The future," Night replied. "For the future is dark, and darkness belongs to the night. The Eve of the Three Moons is coming."
"Three Moons' Night," Rif said. "My b-b-birthday." 
Wayden froze. Rif's birthday was Wayden's too. Could it be a coincidence? 
"Your next birthday will be one you will never forget."
 "Why? Wh-wh-what's going to happen?" Rif asked.
"My grandmother shall be freed," Night said. Storm clouds seemed to swirl in her eyes. They pulsed black for a moment.
"The grandmother of Crag's Grandma . . ." Kolram's thoughts resonated through Wayden's skull. "She must be a Descendant of an Immortal, if she is still alive."
Wayden felt as dizzy as if he were still on the ledge. Mistress Night the descendant of an Immortal?
"The Immortals had many offspring and their offspring many more. I myself had a drop of the Dragonking's blood running through my veins. Back when I had veins."
"How long can Descendants of Immortals live?"
"Have you paid attention to nothing I've taught?" Kolram sighed. "The sons and daughters of Immortals can live thousands of years. Less direct descendants can be hundreds of years old."
"How old are you?"
"I'll never tell."
The Immortals fell from the sky three millennia ago, but no one ever knew where they came from. Wayden had grown up worshipping the Dragonking like everyone else in his family. Kolram claimed the Dragonking was not a God, that the true Gods were above them in the sky. 
Night poked Wayden. "Are you still with us boy? You looked like you were asleep on your feet."
"Sorry, I do that sometimes."
Rif stepped towards Night. "Is there anything we c-c-can get for you?"
Night fixed him with a stare. "Hoping for a kiss, too?" She cackled at Rif's blushing face. "I'm only jesting, boy. No need to do an impression of a tomato. Sing me the song of Night."
Wayden surprised himself by realizing he was rather starting to like the Tulkarian. "Leave him alone. There's no need to make fun-"
Sweet notes emerged from Rif's lips. Wayden's mouth fell open as he listened to Rif sing a beautiful, haunting ballad without a trace of his stutter or Tulkarian accent:

Fire burn with your dark light
Shine your soul, shine tonight
Bring her back, bring back mother
Mother mine, Mother Night

Stolen she was, stolen from me
For the sake of men's hatred, for the sake of their greed
Mother mine, Mother Night
Bring her back, back to me

Robed in red, robed in gold
Day is harsh, the gaudy light
Mother is cloaked in the shades of the dead
The stars stitch silver with a mysterious thread
Bring back my fire, bring back my night

Night sighed and closed her eyes. "Lovely. I'll have that stew, now."
Wayden passed the bowl. He'd heard that song before sung by a bard. He'd always thought the song more Kaldian than Tulkarian because of its reference to the dead. I wonder how Rif knew it.
"Why were you on the l-l-ledge?" Rif asked.
"The stars can speak as loudly as water," Night answered.
"I'm sorry your grandson l-l-locks you in here," Rif said.
"The door isn't locked, and what's so bad about here? I have the stars and the rats to keep me company. It's as good a place as any to wait. My grandmother waits too, beneath the Earth, trapped by Guardians." 
"Who is your grandmother?" Wayden asked.
 Night stepped over to the closed window and placed her palms against the glass. "Once I waited for months until my mother returned. Waiting. We are all waiting. Waiting for a prince to come." She dropped her voice to a whisper. "My prince is death." 
"What do you m-m-mean?" Rif asked.
She turned back towards Wayden and Rif, smiling, the candlelight dancing in her eyes. "Here I have fish stew and sleep powder. My grandson is a fool. He denies his heritage. But I make use of him. He attempts to please those holy men, the Fire Whisperers. He thinks one day he'll join them at the Court of Flame. He dreams of a veil, when he could have the world. I have faith in shadows. The shadows are coming. My grandmother will be awoken. And you, boy," she gestured at Wayden, "will do the awakening."
Wayden's stomach twisted. Did Night mean the Shadow Queen? Wayden would not awake that evil friend of wraiths. So why did she foresee that he would? He knew from his experiences with Mavik's watercolors that prophecy was real. But Night's behavior was so erratic. What was true prophecy and what were just the ravings of a mad woman?
 She yawned and rubbed her eyes. "Ah, leave me boys; I feel sleep coming on. Perhaps my prince is sleep." 
Rif and Wayden left, bearing dirty dishes and the foul smelling chamber pot. Night's voice resonated in the dark stairwell behind them.
"Mother is cloaked in the shades of the dead."
Rif turned to Wayden. "Whooo. Is it always so . . . interesting . . . around here?"
A notch later, after washing the dishes and chamber pot, it was bedtime. Rif and Wayden headed to the sleeping quarters, a plain room with rushes laid on the hard wood. Several of the boys lounged amidst the fibrous reeds. Big Darius threw a mumbly ball against the wall, but Cursed Darius caught it and shouted, "Mumbly!" Big Darius swore, and reluctantly handed over a copper to Cursed Darius. 
Cursed Darius pocketed the ball with a smirk. "Who's cursed tonight, huh?" 
Rif looked around. "Where are the sleeping p-p-pallets?"
"No pallets," Wayden said.
Rif's eyes widened. "Don't they give us bl-bl-blankets?"
Big Darius pushed Rif's shoulder, none too gently. "Don't they give us bl-bl-blankets?" Big Darius's voice was a high-pitched mockery of Rif's. "Shut yer yap, yah wraithin' Tulkarian."
Wayden wanted to come to Rif's defense, but he'd been beaten severely by Big Darius on more than one occasion. The boy was bigger and stronger.
"Leave him alone." One of the orphans, Kazor, stepped between Rif and Big Darius.
 Kazor was as tall as Big Darius, but not nearly as bulky. 
Big Darius shoved Kazor backwards a step. "You a Tulkarian-lover? Got yourself a new boyfriend?"
Kazor shoved back. The Dariuses swarmed around chanting, "Fight! Fight! Fight!" 
Rory's voice called through the ceiling: "I don't wanna be hearin' a peep. You make me come down there, and you'll be regretting it."
Big Darius ran a finger across his throat, then turned away, off to his corner. 
Rif approached Kazor, his eyes glistening. "Th-th-thank you. That was very br-br-brave."
"Don't think anything of it," Kazor said. He looked pointedly at Wayden. "Any decent person would have done the same." 
Wayden flushed. He'd done it again. Just like when he'd stood by while Quiet Darius was beaten up by Big Darius.
Kazor, Little, and Quiet Darius had stopped speaking to Wayden after that. They were right. He should have tried.
"For shame, young Wayden. You stand there and do nothing," Kolram said.
Wayden's stomach tightened. "What is it you want me to do? You might be some great Grandmaster, but I can't cast a spell, or they'll throw me in prison. I'm no one, alright?"
"You waver back and forth from believing you can defeat the Sky Raiders single-handedly and thinking you're nothing at all," Kolram said, "The truth lies in between. Everyone is important and we all play a role in the events that unwind around us. Whether yours will be for good or ill, remains in your hands.
	"I'm no hero. I'm a coward," Wayden thought.
	"If you want to be a hero, be one," Kolram said, "There is no secret to it."
	Rif drifted off after a while, but Wayden couldn't sleep. A draft seeped through the wooden floorboards. Without blankets, the orphans squished together for heat. They teased each other about it, but all ended up huddling. For Wayden, this meant sometimes he set off Kolram's Glimpser powers. Wayden relived whatever memory the Glimpse happened to pick up on-usually not the pleasant ones. 
As a rule, Wayden guarded against touching, but the connection he felt with Rif made him curious. If he touched Rif, would the Tulkarian wake, and wonder what Wayden was up to? If he didn't though, he might never get the answers he desperately sought. Wayden decided to chance it. His fingers touched the Tulkarian's wrist. He let his mind swirl with Rif's, until he felt he was the Tulkarian.
Rif and his mother walked through a row of shops in the Mercy, bearing wicker baskets overflowing with wet laundry. The light from the yellow and orange moons intermingled, reflecting off the wet cobblestones. 
Rif's mother hummed a Tulkarian tune, a haunting melody. Freckles dotted her high cheekbones. It was a busy day, and they had ended up washing the laundry after dinner. Rif didn't want to bother, but his mother couldn't abide wearing dirty clothes. "Just because we haven't much coin, doesn't mean we have to dress in our own filth."
 They passed the Purple Dragon's Inn. The scent of tobacco and whiskey filled the alleyway. Cracked glass sprawled across the window like strands of a spider's web. Through the broken window, someone struck the opening chords on a drum harp to The Dragon and the Princess. 
Rif's mother shook her head. "I have gotten used to their food, but I'll never get used to what the Helesians call music. It sounds like a cat being gutted."
Rif liked some Helesian music, but this bard's singing was as out of tune as his harp. The crowd in the pub shouted, "Hey!" at the end of every fourth line.
A large, smelly drunk staggered out of the bar, the reek of alcohol strong on him. He flicked a matted tangle of dirty blond hair away from his protruding forehead. His stained robe dragged along the ground as he stumbled towards Rif and his mother. 
The knight he came to save the lass
and shoved his lance up the dragon's. . .
"Hey!" the crowd in the tavern shouted.
Rif and his mother turned left at a junction, and the song faded. He glanced behind him. The drunk followed, leering with bloodshot eyes. Clenching the laundry basket, Rif's knuckles whitened.
"Hey! Come here, you Tulkarian whore!" the man shouted. "Come here. I've got something for you."
Rif's mother whispered, "Walk faster. Don't look back."
They turned a corner at the tanner's shop and headed toward the darkened street that lead to their home. The drunk charged forward, a jagged knife in hand. As Rif screamed, the man grabbed Rif's mother and put the knife against her neck. "Lock thy lips boy, or I'll slit her open."
The laundry spilled into a puddle on the muddy cobblestone. Their linens transformed from white to scummy brown. 
 "Help me, Arth." Rif thought frantically.
His skull itched and his head throbbed, the telltale signs that the Grandmaster was about to communicate. A deep voice sounded from inside Rif's mind, "Kill him first. Suck his soul out."
"I can't," Rif thought. " I've never killed anyone."
"Come on, darling," the drunk said. "Into the alley. We'll have some fun, me and you."
Rif's mother tried to pull loose, but the drunk held her firm.
"Go Rif," his mother said. "Run. I'll be fine." The terror in her eyes betrayed her lie.
"You have to cast the spell," Arth said, "He'll kill her if you don't. Don't kill him if you're afraid to, you can use the spell to render him weak, then return his spirit." 
Soul-stealing magic could go out of control if Rif wasn't careful. Anger and fear made it temperamental. He might end up killing his own mother by mistake. If he didn't though . . . there was no option . . . he had to try.
Rif tapped his fingers against his temples, he chanted, "Beast-like man with your steel tooth, let your soul and core come loose."
The drunk froze in mid-step, dropping the knife and gripping at his own throat as if he were choking. A white mist arose from his pale, blue lips and clung there. His eyes bulged and he gagged. A body needed its soul, as much as it needed oxygen. The man slumped to the ground. 
 "Finish him," Arth said.
"I can't."
"You're weak." Arth's voice sounded different to Wayden. Deeper.
"Leave him alone, Arth," A woman spoke in Rif's mind. "Knock him out, but don't kill him, Rif."
Rif let the soul slide back into the unconscious man, glancing around. Had anyone seen him using magic? The street was empty. He and his mother hurried home, fighting hard to breathe.
	The Glimpse began to fade, but Wayden didn't let go of it. He was fascinated. Another soul host! He wanted to learn more, though it could be dangerous to Glimpse too deeply into someone's mind. Again, he chanced it.
The scene of the memory switched, like a dream moving to a new location. 
It was an autumn afternoon. Rif gathered firewood by a pine grove, uphill from his cottage. Fallen pine needles crunched beneath his worn leather shoes. A girl with plaited red hair was gathering wood as well. She smiled at him, an innocent smile. 
Everything went dark. Rif opened his eyes. He was in a different part of the woods and the sky had turned gray. 
Rif must have had one of his blackouts. They'd happen sometimes, especially when he saw a pretty red-haired girl. He wondered if there was something wrong with him, but they were too poor to hire a healer. It had been a long blackout, for it was now at least a notch later.
Rif finished gathering the wood and headed home. He was nearly back when the clouds burst, a downpour soaking him. He rushed into their hovel, where his mother already had a cauldron boiling on the hearth.
 "You're wet as a water weasel!" His mother opened a chest, and fetched him a dry robe. "What took you so long? I was worried."
He stripped off his damp robe. The rain hammered against the roof and a flash of lightning illuminated their hide-covered windows. 
 "I bl-bl-blacked out again."
They sat on the floor with their bowls of soup. 
 "Perhaps we need leeches to bleed you?"
"I'm f-f-fine," Rif said, shuddering at the thought of leeches.
A loud knock sounded on their pine door. "Open up, in the name of the Dracon, ruler of Helos, the Red Land of Fire and Light."
Terror gripped Rif. They know I used magic.
His mother opened the door and a cold wind made the hearth-fire dance. She spoke the common tongue with a strong accent, "What you want?" 
A flash of lightning illuminated a sallow-faced officer. He was garbed in a red surcoat, which marked him as a high-ranking Flame. Behind him, followed half a dozen soldiers, marked as ordinary Flickers by their orange capes. 
One of the Flickers held a magic eater on a leash. The creature was slug-like, about the size of a fox. It slithered along the ground like a snake, tentacles on its head writhing. Source energy drained from his body. It felt like the air was being sucked from his lungs.
 "They're dampening my power." A hot lick of terror shot up his spine. "They're going to arrest me."
Their attacker from the other night stood behind the soldiers. He was no longer drunk, but clean shaven and dressed in a fresher robe, perhaps cleaned by the recent rain fall. He pointed a finger at Rif and his mother. "That's them! They used magic on me for no good reason. I were just being friendly."
The Flame adjusted his helm. "Alright. Get lost, slime."
"What about me reward? I reported unauthorized magic, I did."
The Flame raised his cudgel threateningly, "Here's your reward. Now get lost, or I'll give it to you." 
The man scurried off.
The Flame grabbed Rif's mother's arm and pulled her out into the rain. "Tulkarian, you stand accused of practicing unauthorized magic. By authority of the Dracon, you are under arrest."
"No!" Rif rushed out into the rain and towards the Flame. "Pl-pl-please. It wasn't her. It was me."
"No, son," Rif's mother said. "Don't lie to protect me. Of course it was me."
"N-n-no. She's lying to protect me!"
The Flame laughed. "You a mage, boy? You think because we're soldiers we don't know it takes decades to learn magic?"
"It was nice of you to try to protect your mother," A dark-haired Flicker spoke softly. 
"You should arrest that m-m-man not either of us. He was going to kill us," Rif pleaded. "He took a knife to my mother!"
The Flame waved his gauntleted hand. "Your mother will receive a full and fair trial at the Court of Flames. You two, take the boy to the Home for the Unadoptables. No one will adopt a Tulkarian." The Flicker turned to the other two Flickers. "You two take the woman to the Red Palace."
Flickers tied ropes around Rif's and his mother's wrists.
A bald Flicker asked, "What if the boy is a wizard?"
"A child wizard? Even if he was, it wouldn't matter," the Flame said. "No one can cast a spell with their hands bound."
The Flicker shrugged. "I don't fancy being turned into a toad."
"Why not? It'd only improve you."
Hoof beats thundered from around the corner. A female Flicker with blonde hair rode up on horseback. She rode up so fast that for a moment Rif feared she would trample them, but the horse came abruptly to a stop. "The Striker has killed again, Sir . . . a red-haired girl . . . no marks on her body. Down by the pine grove."
The pine grove, Rif thought with dismay. Was it the girl I saw? She smiled at me. She seemed so nice and pretty.
"Wraithin' hell. Take me to the corpse. The rest of you carry out your orders. Move," the Flame ordered.
Rif was forced into the back of the wagon. The dark-haired Flicker manacled himself to Rif. The rain stopped, but Rif was wet and shivering. 
Rif's mother was thrown in the back of a different wagon. Tears welled in Rif's eyes.
"Mother," he croaked. 
"Be brave, Sweet Possum," Rif's mother called to him as her wagon pulled away from his. "Be strong."
The Glimpse passed, and Wayden opened his eyes. "Poor Rif. He's been through as much as me, in his own way."
"Interesting!" Kolram said. "Rif hosts Grandmaster Arth. Arth practiced soul-stealing, the same magic the man with the mole nose was using in that alley."
"There's someone like me! Except . . . you don't think . . ." Wayden's stomach twisted, thinking of the red-haired girl. "Is he . . . ?" 
"A murderer?" Kolram finished Wayden's thought.
"Rif blacked out and a corpse turned up with no marks on it. Soul-stealing wouldn't leave marks, would it?"
"Correct, but does Rif seem the murdering type to you? He didn't even want to kill the drunk who attacked them."
"Hmmm?true?" Wayden chewed his lip. "There was a woman inside Rif as well. I wonder who that was. Could that have been one of his murder victims?"
"We mustn't jump to conclusions."
Wayden thought for a moment. "Perhaps Rif wasn't doing it intentionally. He has Arth inside him. Could he have taken over Rif, when he blacked out? Could Arth be the Striker?" 
"Rif or Arth could have stolen your soul on the dark stairwell."
"Maybe he only didn't because I'm not a girl."
"Perhaps. But again - does Rif seem like the murdering type to you?"
"No. But the Striker's victims might not have thought him a murdering type either." 
Wayden rolled back over and stared at the mass of shifting shadows dancing on the rafters. 

Chapter 4
The Red Screw

Ah, that clears it up then. I'm a red screw. - Wayden

-<>-  

Wayden had only just fallen asleep when the clanging of Cook's breakfast bell roused him. Through tired eyes, he watched the rest of the orphans leap up and race for the stairwell. The larger Dariuses shoved the smaller ones out of the way. 
Pulling rushes from their hair, Wayden and Rif stumbled behind them. A queue formed stretching from the base of the stairs to the table upon which Cook and Dumb Darius served bread. The boys waited in line, watching the best pieces being snatched. When Wayden and Rif finally had their turn, they stared in disbelief at the food Cook handed them.
"Toast for Toast and . . . purple mold bread for the Tulkarian." Cook laughed.
Charcoal crumbs fell from the crust as Wayden bit into the burnt bread. 
Kazor walked over to Rif. He broke off two corners of his rye and handed one to Rif and the other to Wayden. He shrugged off Wayden and Rif's grateful expressions. "Cook gave me a big piece." 
"Why are you being so k-k-kind?" Rif asked, holding his bread corner as if it were holy.
Kazor smiled. "Being kind is all I got left." 
Wayden blushed. He wanted to be a better person, but he kept slipping back into cowardly habits. "I hope one day I can be like Kazor."
"Hoping is the first step to making it so," Kolram replied.
"Kazor. Thank you," Wayden said.
Kazor narrowed his eyes at Wayden. "I didn't do it for you."
Wayden's cheeks tingled as a tremor of shame passed through his body.
	Wayden had hardly finished his corner of rye when Cook handed him a bucket. "Toast, refill the basin!"
	Wayden headed into the yard, passing Anaz who was returning. The Ozac brushed against Wayden's robed arm. Wayden almost wished he had touched his skin, then he might have Glimpsed important information. It wasn't easy to forgive and forget that the gray man had stalked him yesterday.
"What do you think Anaz's game is?" Wayden headed towards the back of the orphanage, bucket swinging. "Why doesn't he just turn me in to the Fire Guard?"
"You might need to employ a method most desperate: asking," Kolram responded. "Though my anatomy is gone, I am a fellow male, and therefore understand your hesitation to debase yourself by using primitive methods such as communication."
Wayden reached the well and tied the bucket to the rope. "Why would Anaz reveal his intentions just because I ask?"
"Why would he reveal them if you don't?"
After fetching the water, Wayden left Cook and headed upstairs. Crag, Cook, Rory, and Anaz each had their own chambers on the third floor. The Ozac occupied the room that had belonged to Night's former maid.
"Communication." Wayden shook his head. "Like this will work."
Wayden's skepticism turned to curiosity at the sound of a woman's voice from behind Anaz's door. 
 "Both of them? Here?" The woman's voice had the high-pitched warble distinct to Mantus. 
"The Gods above have indeed blessed us!" Anaz said.
"There is no mistake this time? You are sure?" the Mantu asked.
"He looks exactly the same," Anaz said. "Except. . ."
"Except what?"
"In my vision, the boy didn't have a burn mark, but I only glimpsed one side of his face."
A prophecy about me? Was that what brought Anaz here?
"And the other?" the Mantu asked.
"A short Tulkarian boy, with seashells braided in his hair. And the Fire Guard brought him right here for me."
"The boy who will breathe out souls. It can't be a coincidence."
 Crag's door, a few steps away from Wayden, rattled and began to open. There was no time to run away. 
 Master Crag stood in the doorway. "Spying, Toast?" 
With one hand, Crag grabbed Wayden's wrist and squeezed. Wayden glimpsed for a moment Crag at the Fire Temple eyeing one of the Charred Sisters who was covered in nothing but coal dust. Wayden fought off the Glimpse, trying to keep his head clear.
"I was just. . ." Wayden said. I was just what? Come on, Wayden think.
Then Rory's door swung open. "What's going on?"
Rory! I've never been so glad to see him. Wayden waved friendly at the scowling orphanage worker. "Master Rory. Didn't you want me to come write and deliver your love letter?"
Rory's face turned crimson.
Crag let out a loud laugh, releasing Wayden's wrist. "A love letter." He slapped his side. "A love letter!"

-<>-  

That afternoon, Wayden was sweeping the main room when Healer Berik and his daughter, Verica, arrived for one of the healer's monthly visits. Berik's long red curls danced as he strutted into the room.
"Ah, welcome, Lordship. Mistress Night hasn't been doing well lately," Cook said, pushing open the knob-less door to the scullery. 
Berik ducked as he followed Cook into the kitchen, leaving Verica and Wayden alone. 
Wayden swept near the scullery door, eavesdropping on Berik and Cook's conversations. Berik droned, "The dosage must be increased gradually, not more than-"
"Trust me, what they are saying isn't that interesting" Verica threw a leathery ball into the air and caught it. "I've heard it a thousand times."
"What - I wasn't-I-."
"Of course you weren't." Verica's face was so emotionless he couldn't tell if she was being sarcastic or not. "You're about my age aren't you? Fifteen?"
Wayden nodded dumbly. This was the first time he had gotten a good look at Verica, as the few times she had accompanied her father, he'd been on rush-gathering elsewhere.
Beneath her curly red bangs, Wayden glimpsed Verica's scar. That dark gash had been the source of more speculation than the Striker himself. The scar seemed to flow at times like viscous tar.
Big Darius suspected Verica was a devil, while Handsome Darius proposed a wraith possessed her. Rory claimed the scar had to be due to black magic, whereas Crag argued Kaldian science. Wayden didn't know what it was, but it sure made his skin prickle.
The boys had been warned not to mention the scar, not even to look at it. One of the former orphans, Stiff Darius, had called her a freak in front of Healer Berik and was put out on the streets.
"Don't stare at it," Wayden told himself. "Don't stare at it."
	The girl met his gaze, a half-smile tugging at her lips. "What are you looking at, burnt boy?"
	Wayden's cheeks grew warm. "Sorry."
	"It's alright. What's your name? Burnt Darius?" Verica threw him the ball.
	He caught it. "Wayden. I'm from Templeton."
  Wayden tried not to look at the scar, but his eyes gravitated there of their own accord.
"Well, Wayden. Fair trade. You want to know how I got my scar, and I want to know how you got that burn mark." She threw the ball to him again. This time he missed it and rolled into a corner.
He didn't like talking about it to a stranger, but he supposed she didn't either. It was worth it to know. He sighed and threw the ball back to her.
 "When I was little Sky Raiders pillaged my town and torched my home," Wayden said, keeping his face stone.
"What about the burn mark?"
 "My mum and I escaped through the back door." His voice betrayed him for a moment, warbling weakly. "A burning chunk of the rafters fell on me as we ran through the scullery."
"That's awful. I'm sorry."
"What about you?"
	 "There was an accident. When I was little. An explosion in my father's laboratory. They had to stitch me back together."
	"But why is it black?" The words leapt out of Wayden's mouth before he could stop them. "I've never seen a scar like that." Wayden wondered if he'd gone too far. Would he be joining Stiff Darius out on the street?
 "My father used advanced Kaldian techniques to save me."
	 "Impossible," Kolram said.
	 "I thought in Kaldia they had all sorts of things we don't have: fireworks, wind-up clocks, wood prints-"
	"No, this is something far beyond fireworks."
	Wayden's thoughts were interrupted by the scullery door swinging open. Healer Berik emerged from the scullery just as Crag wobbled down the stairs, a half-empty bottle of brandy in his hand. 
	"Ah, Lord Healer Berik! Glad you could finally make it!" Crag took a chug from his bottle and let out a belch. "Did you get the dosages worked out? My grandma is acting even stranger of late - ranting about soul mists and Asgaroth and Three Moons' Night and all sorts of things. We've been through more maids than half the gigolos in Helos."
"And she keeps mentioning the Shadow Queen!" Cook said. "Says she's descended from her!"
Crag paled, feigning a weak laugh. "Heh, heh. So crazy!"
	Verica perked up, wide-eyed. "What did she say about Asgaroth? About Three Moon's Night?"
 "This is not your concern, Verica," Berik said. 
"But Papa-"
"Mind your place, daughter. Adults are speaking."
Verica reddened. 
Berik proceeded into a diatribe about dosages and medications. Verica appeared to be deep in thought. 
Why would Verica be riveted to attention by mention of Asgaroth and Three Moons' Night? He needed to find a way to speak further with Verica in private. But how? 

-<>-  

	The next day, Wayden went again to speak to Anaz. This time, although he hesitated at the threshold expectantly, he overheard no conversations. 
If I want to be a hero, be a hero. He knocked. He hoped he wouldn't regret this.
	"Come in," Anaz called.
	In the center of the room was a hanging sculpture, a mobile. It consisted of a hodge-podge of wires, screws, bolts, plaster, linens and strips of metal crisscrossing in every direction. Shaping tools, measuring cups, and buckets littered the floor. Anaz sat at a desk looking over a tome.
	"Ah, Wayden," Anaz said. "Greetings, young one." 
	Wayden feigned calmness, hiding his trembling hands in his pockets. "Good evening, Sir." Now how to get him to talk? Use a lie to catch a liar. "Is your sister still visiting?" 
	Anaz squinted at Wayden. "My sister?"
	"Well, I assume she was your sister. I heard you speaking with a woman the other day." Now to see if he takes the bait.
	"Ah, yes, you heard that. I thought I heard a mouse creeping outside my door. The orphanage does have ears and wagging tongues. No, my sister had to return to the beautiful snowy plains of Raslo. Only passing through, alas."
	Got you. "Ah, so your sister is a Mantu?  Is your mother an Ozac and a Mantu then?"
	Anaz narrowed his eyes, stepping over to a peg where his sword belt hung. "You try me, boy. She is my half-sister, and a half-Mantu, if you must know. Who are you to question me like the Helesian Fire Guard?"
	He can't frighten me with that big sword. He said himself, if he wanted me dead, he'd have done it by now. "Why did you follow me?"
	"Why would I be following an orphan boy?" Anaz asked, fingering the hilt. "I simply happened to notice you had gotten yourself into trouble in the alleyway and decided to help you out. Is that so wrong?"
Erect as many walls as you like Anaz, I'll just keep pushing.
	"Just helping me out. I see. Probably a coincidence that you showed up here right after and took a job for no pay."
	"I'm not your enemy, Wayden."
	"You're not my friend either."
	"No. . . I'm not your friend. But you serve my purpose. Observe this mobile." 
Wayden studied the sculpture. The mobile consisted of a hodgepodge of materials, mostly metal, and wires, arranged as balances and counter balances.
	"It's beautiful." 
Anaz gestured to the mobile and tapped a tiny, red screw at the center of the wires. "This is you."
	Wayden threw up his hands. "Ah, that clears it up then. I'm a red screw. I had thought I might be a blue nail for a while, so thanks."
	Anaz laughed. "Wayden, Wayden, Wayden. There is a time for talking and a time for listening. I'll say this once." Anaz leaned in, his red eyes and gray skin inches from Wayden. His breath smelled of fire salmon and onion. "When things are balanced, they must stay balanced. To tell you more would be like loosening the screw." Anaz spun the mobile sending it in a dizzying spiral. "It's a link. Would the sculpture stay in place? Perhaps, but maybe not." He grabbed Wayden by the arm and dragged him towards the door. "I've said as much as I will. Now go."
Another prophet, like his brother Mavik and Mistress Night. They were cropping up like tangle weeds after a rainstorm. What was the good of prophecies anyway? The day prior to the Sky Raider attack, Mavik had painted one of his watercolor prophecies. It was his mother, who clutched a red flower to her dress, a one-eyed vulture with a bloody beak, standing over her.
 They had known something bad was coming, but had that stopped the Skymaster's raiders from destroying Wayden's home, the Tulkarian from firing the arrow? Had it froze the blood that flowed from her wound? Had it changed one wraithing thing? 
Chapter 5
         Coppers and Bronzes

It's there around you. Like air, everywhere, yet easy to miss. - Kolram

-<>-  
	
The next morning the weather warmed. Through the open door, Wayden saw the broad leaves on the maples had donned their brightest red. Sunlight blanketed him and the warmth of it was medicine for his soul. He took a step, following Rif and the other orphans into the yard. 
Rory's beefy hand grabbed Wayden's cloaked arm. "Not you, Toast. The Draconess is coming, we need this place in tip-top shape." 
Wayden's heart sank faster than a rowboat hit by a two-ton boulder. "Me? By myself?" 
Rory leaned towards him and whispered, "Shouldn't have told Crag about me love letters, should you?"
Rif turned around in the doorway, the sunlight painting him with an angelic glaze. "I'll m-m-mop, Sir."
"There's your evil Tulkarian," Kolram said. "Except this one tries to take the arrow for you."
Rory glowered at Rif. "Did I ask ya? Get out in the yard with the others and do the weeding, before I belt your scrawny arse." 
Rif scurried off. 
Wayden, an angry fire ablaze in his chest, grabbed the mop. He swabbed the floor as if it were an enemy that needed bludgeoning. "This is Rory."  He thrust again. "Gar Skymaster." And again. "The Ozac that killed Nanny. The Tulkarian archer. Anaz. Crag. Rory, Rory, Rory. Why aren't I allowed outside? Why does life always give me the burnt slice of toast?"
"Rif tried to take the mopping duty for you," Kolram said, "Kazor stood up for Rif. 'Tis true, some are cruel, and more apathetic, but do not lose sight of the beauty in this world."
"Beauty? You see beauty in this place?"
"It's there around you. Like air, everywhere, yet easy to miss. It's in the ruby-red leaves on the maples, the patterns in its bark, in the caress of the sunlight. It's in Rif and it's in you."
"All I see are burnt slices of bread, Cook's warts, and dingy floorboards."
Wayden thrust the mop again, this time imagining smashing Kolram with it. Too bad Kolram was already dead. Or was he? He was awfully talkative for a dead guy.
"I suppose I need a new category for my status," Kolram mused.
"Mostly dead? Life challenged?" Wayden suggested.
"Elegant suggestions. I thank you heartily. Perhaps Kolram will suffice for the present."
"That's it! Kolram the stiff!"
"No."
Teasing Kolram, and the exertion of mopping, improved Wayden's mood a drop, which was more than it did for the floor. "Master Rory, this isn't getting any cleaner. Can I get some more soap?"
Rory mimicked in a soprano, "Master Rory, can I please get some soap? Y'ain't in your manor anymore. Put some muscles into it, Lord Toast!" 
Wayden put muscle into it: This is Rory. This is Rory. And this is Rory. Perhaps Rory's next love letter might read differently than dictated. 'Dearest Honey Suckle. You look like an Ozac's back end.' The idea made Wayden smile.
Master Crag sauntered in, chewing tobacco. His brand new, quadruple-brimmed hat adorned with pink feathers teetered on his head. Wayden bit back a laugh at Crag's fashion statement. 
Crag glowered at Wayden. "What are you staring at, Toast?" 
"An excellent question," Kolram said. "What in the world are we staring at?"
"A pig dressed as a peacock," Wayden suggested. "You know what, you're right, Kolram. There is beauty in the world, and his name is Crag."
Kolram chuckled. "He does make quite a striking figure." 
"Sorry, Sir," Wayden said aloud. "Just admiring your hat."
 "Well, I suppose I can't fault you for that," Crag said, adjusting the angle of his hat first one way, then the other. "But there is no time for hat-gazing. The Draconess is coming," He hung his pink-feathered atrocity on a hook. "Clear the mugs, wipe the table, and lay a cloth. This place better be spotless when she arrives." 
Wayden shook his head. If his mother had been preparing for a visit from a dignitary half the rank of the Draconess, she'd have hired extra staff a month early to have the manor scoured from top to bottom. Crag had one of his orphans tidying for a few moments before she arrived. And no doubt Wayden would be blamed if the Draconess complained of the slightest bit of mess.
Wayden put the mop away and busied himself tidying. It always surprised him that anyone of the Draconess's stature would bother to visit this dump, but the Draconess seemed to enjoy the chance to show off.
Wayden's favorite part of these biannual visits was that the Draconess brought her step-daughter Jazlyn. There was beauty in the world, and her name was Jazlyn. When Wayden wasn't dreaming of killing Gar Skymaster, he fantasized about sailing down a river in a boat with Jazlyn. He would run his fingers through her long, red curls and kiss the freckles on the back of her neck.
Wayden was examining the room, to see if he'd missed a spot, when the front door opened with a bang.
 "Her Royal Highness approaches, Sir," Rory announced breathlessly.
Crag donned his multi-brimmed hat and straightened the lacy collar of his doublet. 
Rory held the door open. A drummer beat his tenor drum and a trumpet blared. 
A Herald entered and tapped the butt of his staff on the ground. "All hail her radiance the Draconess, daughter of Kero, granddaughter of Borleat, first lady of the Drakingdom of Helos, the Red Land of Fire and Light." 
The Draconess swept into the room. Her long, auburn hair swayed against the back of her scarlet silk gown. Her brown eyes were highlighted with henna eye-shadow. It was a wonder she could see where she was going, for she kept her cleft chin aloft. Behind her, trailed the lengthy train of her gown, carried by two young, pretty maids. 
Crag and Wayden sank to their knees. Rory, while holding the door with his back, moved his fingers to lips, forehead, and finally heart. "May the fires guide you."
"May your ember glow in the darkest of nights," the Draconess replied, moving her fingers in the reverse order.	
A line of guards followed the Draconess into the room, the lead one bearing a banner with a dragon on it. The chief of the guards was Sir Cay Longreach, a Royal Companion, one of the Ten, the illustrious Dragonknights. His long blond hair was tucked into a serpent-head silver helm and an ornate hilt rose from his scabbard. It would be something to ride with the likes of Sir Longreach and Swiftrider. Perhaps one day Wayden would ride with them to Raslo and return with the head of Gar Skymaster.
The last guard to enter gave Wayden chills, an Ozac much larger and a darker gray than Anaz. The nightmarish memory resurfaced: the Ozac chasing them into the smoky scullery, Nanny's blood dripping from his mace.
Except for the eyes, this Ozac could have been the other's twin. He was hairless and huge, arms as thick as logs, a tree-trunk sized neck, and a body that could pass as a boulder, but his eyes were calm and green as the Tulkarian Sea.
The herald tapped his staff again. "All hail Princess Jazlyn, daughter Draconi, daughter of Niar, granddaughter of Lochaius, blessed be her embers." 
Jazlyn entered, and fear gave way to wonder. Her red hair spiraled in plaits like a ginger crown. Her golden gown flared outwards at the bottom. Her eyes were sapphires and her freckled cheeks were adorned with henna.
Wayden sighed. "Jazlyn always looks so elegant." 
"But she wears no hat at all," Kolram said. "She needs more brims."
"Jazlyn could even pull off Crag's outfit."
"That I doubt."
 Fire-Whisperer Dade and two acolytes in maroon cloaks entered next, all wearing red veils.
Crag pulled a chair out for the Draconess and wiped it with a cloth. "We are, Great Lady, honored by your visit, and humbled by your magnomiouness."
"Magnomiouness is not a word, Crag. I do enjoy coming here, though. I like to see with mine own eyes the good I have wrought."
One of the Draconess's maids gave the chair a second wipe before she sat. Dade and the two acolytes joined them. 
Crag smiled. "Would Your Majesty care for tea and biscuits?"
"From your cook? I think not," the Draconess said. "By the way, what is that ridiculous thing perched upon your head? Would you kindly remove it?"
Crag's smile wilted as he removed his hat. "Yes, Your Majesty. I-I-I wore it to amuse you."
"Very funny, Crag." 
Crag thrust his hat into Wayden's hands. Wayden hung the silky abomination back on its hook. 
The Draconess studied Wayden. "Ah, look at this happy child. It is so good to make a difference in the lives of misfits. Where would this poor boy be, without our most generous patronage? More than likely slitting someone's throat in a dark alley. Oh, I do enjoy coming here, so uplifting, after the most tedious morning of demands upon our coffer."
"I'm afraid we must ask for coin as well," Crag said, with a wide smile. "Silver and gold is what pays the bills, after all."
"Copper and bronze are often sufficient. I pray your request is of a modest nature. The new army commander requested such hordes of treasure that he would have me canceling all galas to buy clunky armor and rusted swords."
"Clunky armor and rusty swords are what he is trying to replace," Fire-Whisperer Dade said. "I don't think Sir Longreach here would care to go into battle with a shield that will buckle."
The Draconess turned to Crag. "Treasurer Dade is another who would sell my jewels and finery. We have a dragon, what more defense do we need? True, Volkanus is ill, but our Beast Tongue assures me he will fare better soon."
"I believe what Beast Mistress Belza said was that she hoped -"
	"Enough, Dade! Hasn't this morning been trying enough without you ruining the afternoon? I should sell your veil and robes and let you parade around naked. Perhaps that would drive sense into you."
Dade sniffed. "My apologies, Your Majesty. Shall we proceed, then? The accounts, Master Crag?" 
"Of course, I have them right here," Crag showed Dade a blotchy, yellow parchment, dotted with jam and coffee stains. Dade and the acolytes studied the papers. "As you can see, we have forty orphans and the price -" 
One of the acolytes produced a scroll of figures. He cleared his throat and said, "Excuse me, if I may." His finger trailed down the parchment. "Our count shows you have seventeen orphans."
Crag stammered, "I-I-I must have . . . miscalculated. I was never clever with numbers."
 Dade's eyes narrowed. "No, I must admit. That was not clever in the slightest."
As Jazlyn edged towards Wayden, she was so close he felt the heat emanating from her body. He knew it was foolishness for him to have a crush on anyone, much less someone of such a high station. Wayden was a penniless orphan, and a scarred monstrosity to boot. But why was she moving towards him? It seemed deliberate.
Wayden was only half-conscious of Dade's voice in the background: "Master Crag. The state of your accounts has not improved since our last visit."
"I'm sorry," Crag said, "I have a new man, Anaz. Very good with numbers. He can help me. It will be better next time, I promise."
Jazlyn whispered from the corner of her mouth, "You, Burnt Boy."
Wayden pinched himself to make sure he wasn't dreaming. "Me?"
Jazlyn raised an imperious eyebrow, but kept her voice soft. "Do you see any other burnt boys around here?"
"No. It's just. . . I didn't expect anyone so beautiful to talk to?. ah. . . I didn't mean to call you beautiful?not that you're not beautiful?.you are beautiful, but-"
"I must request you save your elegant courtesies for a later date, preferably the seventh of never." Jazlyn raised her chin as she spoke. "Show me around, immediately. Take me to Crag's grandmother."
Wayden stared at her blankly. What in the world did she want? Did she think he could just go off with her and show her around? It would rather be a dream come true, but that's all it was, a dream.
The Draconess looked up from the parchments of figures. She stood up, her face puckering into an hourglass shape. "Jazlyn, whatever are you up to?"
"Stepmother, I request that this boy tour me around the orphanage. I would like to see the great good we have wrought here." 
"Have you taken leave of your senses? I absolutely forbid it."
	Jazlyn gave her stepmother a pleading look. "Org can accompany us." She gestured to the Ozac bodyguard. "Wouldn't it be a marvelous thing to allow this pitiful boy a chance to feel special?" 
Jazlyn gestured towards Wayden, and as she did, the tips of her fingers brushed against him. He felt the pulsating flashes that meant the Glimpse was about to fill his mind. He grew dizzy and felt like he was falling. He expected to see Jazlyn's memories. Instead, bizarrely, he found himself in a memory of the Immortal Asgaroth, the Bone King.
