Asgaroth sat inside an ornate palace chamber across a marble table from his handsome, dark-haired son, Dakarth. The two of them studied a game, pewter pawns on a landscape of movable tiles. They sat on tall stools fashioned of bone. Torches hung in sconces along the stone walls. 
Outside, the wind and wolves howled. Only one moon was out, the orange one, and light fell in slivers through a window slatted with iron bars. A servant fed logs to a hearth while another refilled their goblets with wine. While they played, Asgaroth's younger son, Karsgoth painted a watercolor prophecy on a nearby marble table. His pet crow perched on his shoulder. Karsgoth had the same dark hair and eyes as his father and brother.
 Dakarth sacrificed a game piece to empower his wizard to flip a tile, allowing his knight to capture Asgaroth's flag. Asgaroth smiled. "The son surpasses the father."
Dakarth returned the smile. "You taught me well, father."
The crow cawed, as if in agreement.
"I've raised both my sons well," Asgaroth said, gesturing towards Karsgoth. "Dakarth, you're a brilliant tactician and Karsgoth's powers of prophecy surpass my own."
"Speaking of which," Karsgoth said, cleaning his paintbrush. "The Source has painted another prophecy through me." 
The memory dissipated, leaving Wayden dizzy with disbelief. He'd seen Asgaroth's memories - not Jazlyn's. It made no sense. Could she host Asgaroth, as he hosted Kolram? How? Disorientated and confused, Wayden slipped on the still-damp floor, ending up leaning on Jazlyn's shoulder. 
"Get off me!" Jazlyn shouted, pulling away from him. 
 Dragon Knight Cay Longreach rushed over and shoved Wayden against the wall. Longreach drew a sword. "Draconess, shall I end the wretch?" 
Wayden trembled, staring at the tip of the blade.
The Draconess puckered her lips into the semblance of a trout. "The boy is a fool, but it was unintentional. Still, Crag, I've no idea why you have this strange misfit here, terrifying us with fainting spells. Have you been feeding this boy? What do you do with my funds?" She offered her hand to Dade, who helped her up. "And Jazlyn, we shall speak of your behavior in private. We shall take our leave and withhold funds till our next visit. Our acolytes will come again in a week. If you have the accounts straightened out by then, fine. If not, perhaps this Anaz you mention can be the new head of the orphanage."
Wayden wasn't sure he liked that idea. Who knew what the Ozac was up to with his red screws and his mobiles?
	Crag pleaded. "Your Majesty, please forgive me. I had no idea this would happen."
The Draconess sniffed and headed out the door, her parade of servants trailing behind her. Crag followed, still begging her forgiveness. 
	When the door closed, Rory shoved Wayden so hard he slipped again, his world a swirl of confusion, as he landed on the floor.
Chapter 6
The Last Wolf Ride

Always carry a sharp blade, Rory. Wheat isn't the only thing that has ever crawled up from the earth - Anaz

-<>- 

The following evening, on the way back from delivering Night's food, Wayden eavesdropped on Anaz again. Pressing his ear against the wood of the door, he was rewarded by the sound of the Mantu woman's voice.
 ". . . the Tulkarian's consciousnesses and put them in there."
"We can't yet, Queranos," Anaz said. "Not without a Three Moons' Night."
"Then we must be patient. We must help them survive the field."
"At any cost."
Wayden shifted his weight slightly. The floorboards groaned.
"Do you hear something?" Anaz asked. "I swear, if it's that Toast again, prophecy or not, I'll teach him a lesson."
Silently cursing himself, Wayden raced downstairs, and ducked into the orphan's quarters. He slumped onto the rushes next to Rif, who was already asleep.
"What do you think that was about?" Wayden asked Kolram.
"Queranos said consciousnesses- plural," Kolram said. "Do you think she knows about Arth?" 
"How could she?" Wayden wondered.
"They mentioned prophecy, but that would predict the future, not grant access to Rif's mindscape."
"Mindscape?"
"The world I live in is not as blank as you might imagine it. In fact, though I can see and hear what you experience, I also have a world inside your mind with beds, chairs, gardens, and so on. In fact, parts of it looks like exactly like the home you grew up in."
 "Why does it look like my childhood home and not your own?"
"Because I exist in your mind. Your moods sometimes alter it as well. When you are in a darker mood, it storms here."
"Why didn't you ever tell me that before?"
"You never asked."
Wayden lay awake for a notch, thinking things over. He had just faded off when Kolram said, "I've remembered something. About my death."
One of Kolram's memories came unburied. A robed man on a winged wolf delivered Kolram a parchment bearing the Dracon's seal. Kolram broke the wax and read the letter written in a variant of an ancient language that only a few still knew. It bore orders to fly to Dark Fist to join Dracon Niar and Dakarth for an important mission.
"It was Dracon Niar and Ice King Dakarth," Kolram said. "It was them. They summoned me to Dark Fist. The night I died."
Wayden stopped himself from whistling, not wanting to wake the orphans. The Dracon, was bad enough, but Dakarth son of Asgaroth had remained the iron-fisted leader of Raslo for the last two millennium. "You're sure?"
"I'm remembering more, the memories are unfolding. About the last day I saw my wife. The last day we existed in our own bodies."
More of Kolram's memories poured into his mind. The fur of the skywolf rubbed against him. His wife, Solita nestled against him, warming his body even while the cold wind whipped his back. The wolf beat its mighty wings, propelling along bumpy air currents, hundreds of feet above the pine-dotted, icy terrain of Raslo.
To the east, loomed the Kaldian border, marked by the spiky tips of the Ridgeback Mountains. Towards the South, the summit of Mount Despos looked like an anthill in contrast to the great wall of ice that stretched beyond it. The sunset reflected against the wall, splintering into a light show that no Glower Mage could match. The north fell behind them and the west promised more ice.
He snuggled against Solita. He pressed his nose into the back of her hood. She smelled of lilacs.
Dark Fist loomed closer. The temple was aptly named; the tightly clenched fingers of stone forming its walls jutted skyward. Kolram had never seen the bodies of Darius Dragonking or Asgaroth, which were within the inner ring of the temple in Dark Fist. Both Immortals were entrapped by transparent amber fields of Guardian energy. It would be like beholding Gods.
Kolram moved his mouth close to Solita's ear and shouted over the wind, "Isn't Temple Dark Fist the old Guardian headquarters?"
"It still may be," Solita replied. "My teachers claim Centuron and her descendants still live there, deep in the bowels of the earth, making sure their Guardian magic holds the Immortals and wraiths prisoners."
"Centuron isn't beloved in Helos for imprisoning the Dragonking."
"The Raslonians are no happier about the imprisonment of Asgaroth. Yet, the war between Dragonking and Asgaroth was killing thousands. They had to be stopped."
"Asgaroth started the war," Kolram replied.
"'He started it?' The argument of four-year-olds and four-thousand-year olds alike."
"He stole one of Darius's wives." 
"One of his eight wives, whom he loved so much he beat her, and finally killed her. Asgaroth and Lyssa truly loved each other. Darius should have let her go."
Kolram sighed. "Let's not argue. Whoever was at fault, it matters no more." 
 "As you like. Besides, you know I'm right."
Kolram chuckled. "I know you are wonderful, I'll grant you that."
"Flatterer. Do you see those stones ahead?" Solita pointed at four enormous boulders, encircling Dark Fist. One was shaped uncannily like a skull, another like an anvil, the third a maiden, and the fourth a moon.
The wind whipped Kolram's hood off. "They're hard to miss."
"The Anvil is rumored to be the Guardian Gate. The original Order of the Guardians met Centuron there when they were planning to entrap the Immortals."
Kolram saw no sign of a gate or door of any kind. Perhaps it was a secret door, like the ones the Weaver wove.
"Are you supposed to be telling me all this?" Kolram asked. "I thought you Guardians were supposed to be tight-lipped."
"My lips are never tight where you were concerned." She laughed, pressing against him.
"What do you say we forget about the Dracon and Ice King Dakarth's summons and fly off to a more private location?"
Solita laughed. "Would that we could."
The lovers' flight reached its end. Their winged wolf descended through a breach in the eastern wall of Dark Fist. A heart-like sound thumped in the distance. The floor of the room consisted of a granite outer ring and a marble inner ring, separated by a third ring made of glowing Guardian Magic. Their wolf landed on the outer ring.
On the inner ring the other Grandmasters, looking impatient, awaited. A cadre of guards surrounded them, half in the orange cloaks of Flickers, the other half Raslonian armor: bones grafted into black metal. They stood in a semi-circle around a dais. Beneath the dais lay the two Immortals.
Asgaroth lay trapped, sleeping on his bed of stone. The Dragonking held vertically, near Asgaroth, was encased in a separate shield. High cheekbones, a square jaw, dark hair, brooding eyes- a decidedly handsome face. There was little hint of the evil that lay beneath. 
"Why separate Guardian shields?" Kolram helped Solita down from the wolf. Her hands were frozen. He gave them a quick rub. "Why didn't Centuron put them in the same field?"
"They were trapped at separate times and in different fashions," Solita whispered, as they approached the inner ring. "Asgaroth was entombed by a spell using the blood of each type of magi. No one knows what trick Centuron used to entrap Darius."
 "And the Shadow Queen?"
"No one knows where her body is."
"You're allowed to be telling me all this?"
"No. Don't you dare share this information or I won't kiss you for a month. Well, a week. A day at least. Half an hour."
They stifled their giggles as they stepped over the energy snakes, and into the inner ring. Of all the Grandmasters, Arth looked the most miserable. His pale face was even more lined than usual and there were bags beneath his bloodshot eyes. Arth's sister had been killed less than a month ago.
The Grandmistress of Fire-whispering glared at Kolram. "Could we get this over with? I've better things to do than watch Kolram and Solita fawning over each other."
Raslonian soldiers poured through a side door, silencing further argument. One called out, "All bow to King Dakarth, supreme ruler of Raslonia, Land of Ice and Might, wielder of the Sword of Night, First Seat on the Council of Steel, son of Asgaroth, the Bone King."  
Dakarth stepped forward, the spitting image of his father, though the son looked the far older of the two. It made sense, Kolram supposed. After all, he was only the descendant of an Immortal, not an Immortal himself. His black cloak draped over his heavy chain-mesh. His glittering black sword hung from its sheath. Four bone-armored guards stood at his side.
"All bow for Dracon Niar," a gray-bearded Dragon Knight called out. "Lord of Helos, Land of Fire and Light, son of Terok, Possessor of the Red Blade, Wearer of the Crown of Fire, First Seat upon the Court of Flames,  heir to the Dragonking, and Head Whisperer of the Temple of the Third Moon."
Dracon Niar, wearing a golden veil and a white sword at his hilt strode forward. His red-jeweled crown flickered as he walked. Guards in orange cloaks surrounded him.
"Greetings, Grandmasters. I apologize for the delay," Dakarth said, his voice echoing off the crystalline walls. "I promise you tonight will be a night you shall never forget."
A bell rattled. Wayden sat upright, as Cook's shout carried up the stairs. "Breakfast!"
Wayden hadn't even realized he'd fallen asleep. Memories and dreams sometimes bled into each other. He let out a yawn and stretched. "Wraithin' hell, we were just getting to the good part."
"Is the good part my wife and I getting killed?" Kolram asked.
"Aye. Err. I mean no. Kind of. I guess?"
"Impressive, four answers to the same question each more insulting than the last."
"I'm talented that way."
Wayden grabbed a fresh robe, thankful that yesterday had been laundry day and headed down the stairwell wiping sleep from his eyes.
Rory bellowed, "Fifteen-year-olds get served first this morning. The Dracon's crops needs harvesting! Fifteen-year-olds grab toast first and eat in the wagon."
Wayden and Rif may have been at the front of the line for a change, but Cook still handed Wayden a blackened heel of bread. 
Anaz stepped up behind Cook and smiled, revealing his glittering fangs "A good piece today. The boy will need his energy."
Cook jumped, sending his warts jiggling. "Yes, Sir, Master Anaz, Sir. Didn't see you there."
Wayden took the piece of bread, wondering why Anaz had intervened. The orphans went out chewing toast as they walked, and then lined up to clamber into the back of the Dracon's wagon. Anaz passed the orphans and wordlessly clambered on the driver's bench, beside Rory and a silver-bearded driver. 
"Anaz, you don't need to come," Rory said. "Master Crag said I was in charge of this trip."
"So, be in charge. I'm simply here as an observer." Anaz drew his sword and sharpened it with a whetstone. "And if anything should go awry, you'll have a little bit of extra help from me."
"What, you think we might run out of scythes and need your sword?"
"Always carry a sharp blade, Rory. Wheat isn't the only thing that has ever crawled up from the earth."
What did that mean? Did Anaz foresee some approaching danger?
Rory cast Anaz a hard look. "You ain't practicing unauthorized magic with that cauldron of yours, are you?" Rory asked, as he gave the reins a shake. "What type of trouble you seeing in yer water?"
"Of course, I wouldn't practice magic without the Dracon's leave. Just an instinct, and besides, I'm feeling a bit cooped up. It's good for a man's health to get some fresh air every once in a while."
"Alright," Rory said. "Say, Anaz, do you know anything about writing poetry?"
"Yes. I know not to."
Rory mumbled something as the driver tugged the reins. The wagon meandered past the perimeter of Vilanos and entered the countryside. Their wheels crunched brittle leaves. The cobblestones of the road gave way to a river of mud and muck, while overhead the sky formed a thick, gray blanket. Rain would bring a final boost to the crops, but if the temperature dropped early, as the weather witches of the four towers had predicted, snow would smother the last harvest. A lean winter for the wealthy meant an even leaner one for orphans.
   The smell of manure lay thick in the air, as they rode past wattle-and-daub huts, copses of trees, farms, and gardens. A tiny brook trickled alongside the road. A lime-skinned Mantu woman with golden bulbous eyes passed them. Her green hair blew in the wind, as she pulled the shafts of a rickshaw with webbed fingers. A young freckled swineherd steered his sows across the road. Chickens squawked and darted as a red-haired girl chased after them.
Rif stared intently at the girl, an intense expression on his face. The Striker's victims all were redheads. Was it Rif staring at the girl so intently or Grandmaster Arth?
 "What do you remember about Arth?" Wayden asked Kolram.
Kolram focused for a moment, unearthing buried memories. Kolram had followed Arth into a Raslonian tavern, filled with a throng of drunken people. The stench of body odor and tobacco wafted through the air. Wind and snow hammered the window glass, but the tavern's hearth was lit, and a bard played Death and the Sailor's Wife on his drum-harp.
By the time Kolram made his way through the crowd, Arth was seated at a wooden table in the darkest corner of the tavern, nursing a goblet of brandy. Veins like tree roots bulged across his pale-white forehead. His eyes darted back and forth. Kolram suspected Arth was on the brink of insanity, following the recent loss of his sister, but it wasn't concern for the Grandmaster that prompted Kolram to spy on him. It was suspicion about his pupils: the new Dracon and Dakarth. What had these two powerful leaders and Arth been up to with these books of Raylar they were keeping secret? What dark magic were they practicing?  
Kolram approached Arth through the crowd. "May I sit?"
"Wraith off," Arth snarled. 
Kolram remained standing, but didn't leave. "Arth, I heard about the death of your sister. I'm so sorry."
Arth flicked his wrist, showering Kolram with the contents of his goblet.
Brandy dripped from Kolram's chin onto his scarlet robe. The music stopped, and Kolram felt eyes on him and heard whispers of 'mage fight'. 
Arth turned to a tavern-wench, a Raslonian female with indigo hair. "Another glass. Make it two. One to drink, one to throw." 
Guffaws and chuckles mixed with the murmurs surrounding them. The tavern wench scurried off, dark-blue hair bobbing. The bard resumed his strumming. Kolram cringed at the opening notes of The Dragon and the Princess.
He wiped the dripping brandy from his face. "I just wanted you to know that I'm here if you want to talk."
"You don't care an Ozac's eyeball about me or my sister," Arth said, with a bitter twist of his lips. "You want to find out about our new school of magic. You'll learn naught from me, Red-Kingdom spy."
Heat rushed to Kolram's cheeks.
The bard sang, "The knight he came to save the lass and shoved his lance up the dragon's. . ." 
"Hey!" The crowd shouted.
The wagon bumped, jolting Wayden. Kolram's memory had been so visceral, that it was jarring. It was like awaking from a dream, where he thought he was one thing, to awake to find he was something else. Not a Beast-Tongue-Grandmaster-spy after all, but an orphan riding through the country of Helos. Rif was back to his normal smiling self again. 
Wayden mused on what he had learned. "Arth was involved in a secret school with Dracon and Dakarth."
"We know from the Glimpse of Rif that Arth's a soul thief," Kolram mused. "Was that what he was teaching the Dracon and Dakarth?" 
Up ahead, a man struggled with a broken wagon's wheel. A traffic jam of carts stretched back from it. Rory cursed. "This will hold us up a bit. If you need to relieve yourself, do it now." 
 Wayden and Rif leapt from the wagon and headed to a different clump of bushes than the Dariuses. Anaz's eyes tracked him, but the Ozac kept his distance. 
Mist rose where Wayden watered the tree roots. Wayden had learned a lot when he'd risked talking to Anaz. Maybe this communication trick Kolram advocated would work with Rif as well. "Do you have Arth's voice in your mind?"
Rif turned in surprise almost splattering Wayden. "You kn-kn-know?"
"Careful!" Wayden glanced around for prying ears. He whispered, "Rif. I'm a Glimpser. Kolram is in my head."
"W-w-wait. What?"
"I was born with the voice of Kolram in my mind. My twin brother had one too - Jijari, a Water Prophetess."
"You have a twin br-br-brother?" Rif asked, adjusting his breeches.
"In my Glimpse, I heard Arth in your mind, but there was also a woman. Do you have two people in your mind?"
 "Arth's s-s-s-sister was attacked, Arth was there. He used Soul-stealing. H-h-he?"
"All right, they cleared the road!"  Rory shouted, "That's enough watering the fields. Everyone back into the wagon."
Grumbling, stiff, and still sleepy, the orphans climbed back aboard the crowded wagon. A short notch later, the wagon reached the Dracon's field, a rich plot of land that sprawled endlessly with golden shafts of wheat. A score of peasants were already at work: gray-haired crones with stooped backs, dirt-covered men with arms like hairy tree trunks, hefty matrons, and barefoot children who spied from behind sheaves of wheat. Mavik would have made a lovely watercolor of the scene.
They climbed out of the wagon and joined in the harvesting. Wayden had grown stronger, and he found the work less tiring than last year. The peasants sang a working song as they swung their scythes to a reaper's rhythm:
Cut 'em, bind 'em, throw 'em in the wagon
Bag it, tag it, bring it to the mill
Oh whoa, bring it to the harvest
Oh whoa, fire in the mill.
Oh whoa, bring it to the harvest
Oh whoa, fire in the mill

The song, and the work, inspired a hypnotic rhythm. Sweat dripped from their brows despite the cold.
"So what were you saying about Arth's sister?" Wayden asked, as he swung his scythe at a stalk of wheat.
Rif planted the base of his scythe in the ground. "Belok, he . . . wait . . . do you h-h-hear something?"
Wayden listened, trying to filter out the noise of the singing. A faint scraping sound, like insects, clawed beneath his feet, growing louder by the moment. 
"Stop singing," Wayden shouted. "Listen-"
Rory glared at him. "Who are you to-."
Before he could finish, a brown, leathery, pincer-like hand burst through the earth, and grabbed Rif's leg.

Chapter 7 
The Cruelest Claw

Come, Moon Howlers! As fast as fur can fly. -Wayden

-<>- 
 
"Scagazi!" Wayden shouted. Anaz drew his sword. Most peasants scattered while some fought with scythes, hooked blades meeting hooked hands erupting from the earth.
A brown, leathery pincer squeezed Rif's leg, despite his screaming and kicking. Wayden took a breath. 
Think, Wayden, think. What do I know about scagazi that could help? Their exoskeletons made them hard to damage using physical means. Bright lights blinded them, but he practiced no Glower magic. 
"Kolram, can you control them?"
"I cannot control or even speak to scagazi. They are more insect than mammal. I could summon animals from the nearby forest, but then our secret would be known."
Wayden glanced around, but Anaz and the other peasants were occupied in battles of their own. Wayden fought against an urge to flee towards safety, abandoning Rif. Kolram was right: if you want to be a hero, be one. He tightened his jaw. I'd rather die, than be a coward any longer. He lifted his scythe, and with a roar, charged towards Rif. 
A patch of open flesh on the scagazi's wrist caught Wayden's eye. He aimed the scythe, the blade biting into a gap in the exoskeleton. He yanked the scythe loose, ivory blood spraying in every direction.
 Rif broke free, eyes moist. "You s-s-saved me."
I can't believe I did it!
"Stay calm. We aren't out of this yet," Kolram said.
"Stay calm, Rif," Wayden parroted, infuriated at the way his voice came out more of a squawk than a hero's bellow. "We aren't out of this yet."
 Several scagazi burst through the ground. Their narrow faces bristled with spiked hair and their tiny eyes twitched constantly. Anaz swung his sword, but the blade bounced off the monsters thick exoskeleton. One scagazi lunged at Anaz, and he barely managed to dodge to the side. Anaz spun, and swung again, sword whistling through the air. This time it caught a gap in the scagazi's carapace and the monster went down with a crash. Two more charged at Anaz. Grim determination transformed the Ozac's usual sly grin into a scowl. 
"Beasts of Centuron," Anaz hissed, as he swung his sword again. The metal cut deep into scagazi flesh. For each one killed, another seemed to be birthed from the ground.
"We need to get out of here!" Wayden shouted.
A patch of dirt in front of them vibrated and swirled. A clawed leathery pincer sprouted from the earth- then another and another- like a crop of death. Wayden spun, but a scagazi burst onto the surface directly before him. He swung his scythe, but missed, managing only to decapitate a wheat stalk.
 The scagazi let out a shrill cry as it leapt towards him. Wayden turned to run, but twisted over his own feet and tumbled to the earth. He steeled himself for death. 
This is it.
A figure flew through the air, knocking the monster off Wayden. Kazor. I need to find the courage he has. 
As Wayden found his feet, Kazor lost his. The scagazi grabbed Kazor's legs and yanked him into the ground. The kindly orphans eyes widened and he reached out in desperation. Wayden grabbed for him, fingertips brushing Kazor. Churning soil swallowed the boy, leaving a grave of overturned clumps of dirt.
No. He'd been so kind, so good. I deserve to die not him.
"Grieve later." Kolram sent a wave of calming energy through Wayden's body. It didn't take away the pain, but it helped Wayden manage it, keep it in check. "There are wolves nearby."
"If I cast the summons, the other orphans and peasants will see me. I'll be arrested."
"You may have no other choice."
Wayden saw a burly peasant driving off a scagazi in the distance. Then the peasant fell down. Wayden lost sight of him beneath the walls of grain.
The soil simmered, then, as if hitting the boiling point, chunks of dirt erupted skyward - followed by a scagazi. The orphans pivoted right and ran into Big Darius. Another scagazi broke through the soil. They tried to head left, but again were blocked.
 "We're trapped," Big Darius sobbed. "We're wraithin' trapped. We're going to die."
Jagged mandibles clicked as the scagazi slithered closer.
Anaz charged through the grain, sword flashing. He caught one of the scagazi full force. A second and a third fell to his blade. A surge of hope charged through Wayden, until a fourth scagazi grabbed the Ozac's feet. Wayden met Anaz's eyes and the Ozac smiled. "Until we meet again." Then he was pulled underground in a swirl of dirt. 
Was this why Anaz had followed him? Stayed with him? Brought his sword and sharpened it? To give his life to save Wayden's? Why? Even in death the Gray Man remained an enigma.
The scagazi swarmed, mandibles clicking as they encircled Wayden, Big Darius, and Rif. Thick, pointed tails swayed back and forth, as if to some inaudible drum beat. Big Darius swung his scythe, only to have one of the creatures catch the blade and yank it from his hand.
"You have to use your magic," Kolram said.
Wayden swallowed. "You're right."
He dropped his scythe and moved his hands in semi-circles. His mind found a nearby pack of wolves. It was as if Kolram knew their language. They were hunting. They knew about the battle nearby. He filled their minds with the thought of wolf's chomping on scagazi meat.
 Then he chanted. The words for the spell could be anything, but they had to be chanted aloud. Something rhythmic tended to work best. "Come, moon howlers. Beasts of the woods. I am the moon king. I am the sharpest tooth, the cruelest claw. You will come. Come!" 
Big Darius edged backwards. "You- yer a witch!"
The scagazi crept closer.
The pack leader's mind resisted for a moment. Wayden felt like he was another larger wolf snapping at the pack leader. The two wolves circle each other. Than Wayden leapt at the wolf, snapping his jaws on its neck. Its resistance dissipated. It was in his control. 
Something moved in the stalks of wheat. Handsome Darius burst through the wheat stalks, a scagazi at his heels. He rushed towards Wayden, snatching the abandoned scythe from the ground. He turned and swung the blade right into a scagazi's throat.
Handsome prodded at another, keeping it at bay, while Wayden chanted, "Come! With tooth and claw drive the monsters back to ground! Come! As fast as paws can leap! Come! As fast as fur can fly."
The wolves bound through field of grain. Source Energy resonated in his throat and chest, as he continued to chant. 
"I'm not sure the wolves will get here soon enough," Kolram said.
"Well if we die, at least we won't be thrown into prison for using unauthorized magic."
Big Darius stared, wide-eyed, at Wayden. "Please- don't turn me into a newt." 
Wayden shook his head. They were surrounded by scagazi, and Big Darius was worried about that? Becoming a newt might be a step up for the lummox. At least amphibians recognized their enemies.
Hurry, wolves. He felt like he was back at his burning manor, waiting for the Fire Guard and the dragon to drive the Sky Raiders away.
One of the scagazi crawled past Handsome Darius, who was busy fighting off another scagazi. 
We're finished this time.
Rif spread his arms and fingers, gesturing at the scagazi. He sang without a stutter, "Inside your treasure you keep. I take it from you, in one fell sweep."
Mists appeared at the scagazi's mouth. The monster hissed and writhed on the ground. Its mist was thinner than the fogs Wayden had seen coming from the acolyte's lips, or the one Rif had pulled from the would-be rapist.
Rif's eyes went black.
 The scagazi's mist floated off, the creature's wiry body falling still. Wayden had never witnessed anything killed by Soul-stealing firsthand before. The creature's body shook and its dying shrieks sent a shiver up Wayden's spine.
"That was how you died?" 
"It wasn't pleasant," Kolram siad. "There may be more scagazi coming. I suggest-"
Two scagazi cornered Wayden, drawing closer and closer. An acrid scent wafted from them. One of the scagazi leapt on top of him, bowling him over into a bed of wheat. The leathery monster clawed at Wayden's face. Spittle dripped from the creature's mouth.
"Rif!" Wayden called out.
This time, Rif spoke in Tulkarian, as he cast his spell. What he said, Wayden didn't know, but the effect was obvious, as the mist streamed out of the scagazi's mouth. Another scagazi leapt at Rif, but a ball of fur intercepted it mid-flight. Claws clashed with pincers, swirling together to become an amalgamations of Fangs, mandibles, fur, and armor.
 The scagazi was on top of the wolf, ready to deliver the death blow. The pack leader ducked under the blow, surging forward. Her jaws closed in a triumphant chomp. 
A dozen more wolves poured into the clearing, leaping at the oncoming scagazi. A wolf snapped a scagazi's leg, lofting a fountain of white blood. The gray-furred pack-leader tore a scagazi's limbs as if pulling legs off a grasshopper. The few surviving monsters turned face, burrowing for safety, uprooting wheat stalks in their wake. The wolves dragged the corpses into the woods.
"Thank you," Wayden communicated to them. "You do honor to the pack." He released his hold on their minds.
The rush of adrenaline deserted him, and he slumped to the ground. The heavens burst open, raining on the weary survivors. 
Tired cheers erupted, mixed with groans of pain and sobbing. A gray-haired woman treated a badly wounded peasant boy. Two other women watched, sobbing.
 A middle-aged man lay dead and two men were tending the body. A woman shrieked for her missing daughter until a man found her hiding under a wheelbarrow. The peasants cheered as the mother embraced her child. Kazor, Anaz, and two farmers were gone.
Wayden clenched his teeth, fighting back tears. He'd failed them.
Big Darius pointed at Rif and Wayden. "You're witches."
We'll be arrested by the Fire Guard. 
 "We didn't d-d-do anything," Rif said. 
Rory inched closer, eying Wayden and Rif.
"You did," Big Darius said. "I heard you muttering strange words and gesturing. You're illegal witches. Unauthorized magic. They be payin' for the likes of you."
Handsome Darius thumped Big Darius's wounded arm. Big Darius winced. "Lock your lips, Big D. You know they been roundin' up magi. Wayden and Rif just saved our lives. Do you want the Dracon to grab them just cause they didn't get no authorization? Why do people need authorization to be saving someone's life? That ain't right."
Rory shook his head like a stubborn two-year-old. "Magic is illegal and that's that."
"Magic? What are you talking about?" Wayden laughed. His insides churned. "I think you've written one too many love letters. I was just praying." 
"That weren't any prayer I ever heard," Big Darius said, fiddling with his bandage. "All them funny words and what you were doing with yer hands. That's witchcraft that was."
Wayden's laugh trembled. It came out sounding more like a strangled duck than a confident rebuttal. Rory's gaze bore into him: suspicion mixed with self-righteous indignation, a dangerous combination.

Chapter 8 
The Nest
	
						 
		Now, you are neither man nor lion. You're a rat. -Mavik

						-<>-	

						Mavik

The icy ground crunched beneath Mavik's worn leather shoes. 
Seven years in the cold, gathering wood for the slaver's fire. Seven years serving as property to the men who burnt his house down and killed his mother. Seven years since Wayden let go of his hand and Goat snatched him. Seven years since Mavik discovered his father was a traitor.
He craned his neck to stare up at the Nest, the circular fortress of towering granite walls that snaked Mount Despos. The bonfire known as the Beacon burned on its summit, a fiery crown adorning the mountain's head. Gar Skymaster, and his Raider minions basked in its warmth, while Mavik and the other slaves slogged through slush.
Mavik pulled a wooden sled past run-down shacks and privy ditches filled with frozen feces. A team of Sky Raiders flanked them; led by the one-eared man named Isel, the man who had torched Mavik's home. One of the Sky Raiders held a collared magic-eater by a chain. The slug-like creature slithered alongside, blocking the potential of the magi using their powers to escape.
Skarak, the Ozac Sky Raider who had killed Nanny, dragged a witch towards the chopping block. Opel, a Glower Witch, had run off in the middle of the night. She was brave, if not wise. She'd already lost two fingers from previous attempts. A necklace of dried fingers hung around Skarak's neck.
Mavik looked away. He didn't watch, but he heard all too well: the blade sharpening on a whetstone, Opel begging for mercy, the thunk of the cleaver descending against the chopping block, Opel's high-pitched shriek, and Isel's breathy laughter.
Mavik stared down at his own fingers. He still had nine of them, all but his left pinky.
Skarak brought Opel, and a healer witch who was cradling Opel's hand in a blood soaked rag, back to the sleeping furs. The other sky raiders escorted Mavik and the others deeper into the pine forest. 
The evergreens, as thin and tired looking as the prisoners, bent in their attempt to survive the bitter wind. The sled's runners crunched dried needles, scraping parallel ravines into the icy snow. The smell of cedar mixed with the faint scent of blood. Slaves wordlessly gathered firewood. Some of the Sky Raiders encouraged work songs, but Isel never did. 
Isel gestured at the sled. "Logs in the back, mid-sized in the front, kindling in the middle."
As if they hadn't heard it a thousand times before. 
They all knew every type of Raslonian wood. The ones to gather (black locust, beech, white ash, pine, and dragon wood) and the ones to avoid (stink sap, mirror wood, iron-bark, poison ash, and tangle thorn). Mavik could write a book on it. A Treatise on Gathering Firewood for a Heartless Dictator, by Mavik, son of Kelsen the Rat. 
Isel turned to Mavik's father and said, "Rat, yer son's slacking. Give him a taste of leather."
Kelsen had been nick-named after his pet, an albino rodent, and the name was apt. It had come as a shock when Mavik found out that Kelsen was at the slave camp. Mavik had known his father had disappeared chasing after Gar Skymaster, but no one knew what had become of him since. If that was a shock, it was a cloud full of lightning to find out his father was now Kelsen the Rat, a collaborator. 
For slightly less rancid food, a private tent, half-decent boots, and a pet rat, his father had deteriorated from the noble Lord of Telek Manor into the Sky Raiders' lap-dog. Kelsen's matted hair and beard hung in long tangles. Even collaborators didn't get razors. His once erect posture had bent into one similar to his rats. Kelsen had been the one to take Mavik's finger. "I'll just take your left pinky. You won't even miss it."
Now his father feigned compassion. "Do as he orders, son. Save yourself some grief. We've been through this before."
Mavik rubbed the nub of his missing pinky. "What are you waiting for?"
Kelsen sighed, and then half-heartedly flicked the whip. Isel tugged the chain on his magic-eater. "Pfff, ye call that a lash, Rat? Do it right or I be doing it meself and give him a right proper taste of pain."
Mavik played his role in a drama that had been reenacted a thousand too many times. The show was entitled: 'In which Isel exercises his need for power, Mavik his stubbornness, and Kelsen reveals his amazing lack of spine.' These were the roles they'd been cast in and they knew no other lines.
Kelsen spun Mavik around and brought the whip down. It was just like Mavik was a little boy again, over his father's knee, except the belt had become a whip. The leather's venom burnt, sending Mavik falling into the snow gasping. 
Kelsen moved to help Mavik up, whispering, "It doesn't have to be like this. Why do you have to be so stubborn?"
Mavik's chest burned with anger. He yanked his arm free from Kelsen's grasp. "They murdered your wife. How does that not bother you?"
Kelsen shook Mavik. "The lion eats the deer. The strong rule the weak."
"Then don't be weak." Mavik found his feet. "You're a man, not a beast. Or were, at least. Now, you are neither man nor lion." Mavik stared at Kelsen's pet rodent, perched on his shoulder. "You're a rat." 
Kelsen brought down the whip in a bout of cold fury. 
"All right, Rat!" Isel shouted. "That be enough! I said whip him, not kill him."
Mavik fought back a sob. It wasn't the lash that stung so badly. Even more agonizing were the constant reminders that his father was gone and only a flesh puppet remained. 
Once Mavik had asked his father why he had become a collaborator. His father replied, 'We're stuck here anyway, son. Join me. Let's make the best of this place.' Tired of cold and pain, which stretched on like the endless icy plains, there were moments when Mavik was tempted.
Jijari, the Splasher witch inside Mavik's mind, whispered to him, "Hold on to your heart, Child. Driftwood finds its way home. We've seen it in the water that your future is not here. It's getting closer. The future comes in on wings."
A shadow fell on Mavik. Looking up, he saw the underside of a gray skywolf spiraling towards him. The vast wings spread like a tent, and beneath its feathered canopy, the skywolf's matted gray fur covered sinewy muscle. The wolf landed. Two figures straddled the beast's back, Goat and another, obscured behind him.
Seeing Goat always brought back painful memories of the day Mavik's home had burned. Wayden shouldn't have let go of my hand, Mavik thought for the thousandth time. 
"The Source wanted it this way," Jijari said. "There is a plan."
"A plan. Some plan this has been."
If Wayden hadn't let go, his life would have played out differently. He wouldn't have mistaken Goat's smoky silhouette for his brother's, wouldn't have called to him, and ran to him.
A powerful sensation, almost as if an invisible cord around his heart had been tugged, yanked Mavik from his reveries. Mavik caught sight of the prisoner behind Goat. His breath caught in his throat at the sight of the blonde-haired girl from his Splasher visions. The tattoo, the filthy blonde hair, the hazel eyes, the cat-like face with the high cheek bones tattooed with swirling vines - there was no mistaking her. And he felt the Connection to her.
"The Source is leading us back together," Jijari said. "There is no doubt about it. She hosts a Grandmaster."
A memory passed through Mavik's mind, of Jijari pounding on Magic-finder Zaburn's door. She breathlessly told him that all the Grandmasters would be separated. She'd foreseen them blown apart, separated like dandelion seeds. The next week all the Grandmasters had been ordered to Darkfist. While gathered on the inner ring, Zaburn quietly cast a Connection Spell on the Grandmasters.
Goat unbound the thick hemp rope, revealing a red ring of flesh where it had bitten into the girl's bony wrists. Kelsen took her arm and led her forward. The girl's eyes locked on to Mavik. She scrunched her forehead and cocked her head at an angle as she studied him. She's feeling the Connection spell too!
What could he say? 'Excuse me. Do you feel an invisible rope tugging at your heart?' He'd come off looking less crazy if he stripped naked and danced with a chicken on his head.
A notch later, his chance to speak to the girl arrived. The Sky Raiders and Kelsen studied a crinkled parchment brought by Goat. Kelsen, the only literate one, read aloud the detailed results from the latest Tulkarian Blood Games.
 Mavik approached the girl, who was shivering in her thin robe.
"You alright?" Mavik asked.
"'Sides bein' taken from my family to a frozen wasteland? Aye, I be dandy."
Mavik removed the fur he wore over his cloak and handed it to the girl.
She threw it back at him with a smirk. "Keep yer clothes on, boy."
"Take it. I took this one off a man who died from the cold." Jijari had told him it was against the Source to filch from corpses, but Mavik ignored her. The dead never complained.
The girl sighed. "Don't be thinkin' I owe you." Her voice was muffled, as she put the fur on. "I'll be payin' you back, just as soon as I find me a corpse."
Mavik started to laugh, but a pang of hunger, coupled with his bruised ribs, doubled him over.
 She put a hand on his back. "You be alright?"
"I got my back scratched with a whip. And I'm hungry. But besides that I'm dandy too."
The girl smiled. "I be Emerelda. I be feelin' you about the hunger. My tribe lives in Deep Woods. There were some winters when I almost ate me own shoes."
"I'd kill for a good shoe right now." 
Emerelda laughed, half-turning away from him and wiping something from her face. When she turned back, Mavik noticed traces of moistness on her pink cheeks. "Sorry. It be a day from the nether world. The raiders caught me out huntin'. I'll never be seein' my Da and tribe again, I reckon."
Mavik wished he could tell her she was wrong, that he'd get her back to her tribe, but the truth was he didn't know. Jijari's visions showed him in a tunnel with her. There was a woman all in white sealed in a Guardian field. There was a golden armored knight. There were woven statues. He hadn't seen anything resembling returning Emerelda to her family. There were always junctures though and the future was never certain.
"I'm sorry," Mavik said, though a selfish part of him was glad she was here. Perhaps it meant that soon he'd run off with this girl and escape the slave camp. Whatever they would face in those tunnels, it couldn't be worse than life in the Nest.
Isel shouted at Kelsen, "Rat, get that girl and Mavik working before they taste me leather."
"He needs a new line. And I'm so hungry the leather is tempting," Mavik whispered. "Perhaps I'll eat his whip. It can't be worse than the food they give us. In fact, I'd hazard they make the food and the whips from the same leather."
Emerelda stifled a snicker, as Kelsen approached, whip in hand. His white rat crawled from his pocket and settled on his shoulder. 
Mavik glowered at his father. "We'll get back to work. Now leave us before I tear that whip out of your hand and choke you with it." 
Kelsen's face reddened. "I'm still your father."
"You're a rat."
Kelsen backhanded Mavik into the snow and stormed off.
Emerelda eyed him. 
"That's my father."
"I be gatherin'."
Isel separated them after that: Emerelda filled the baskets, while Mavik gathered another sled-load of wood. They kept at it until sunset. Even the skywolves were drooping by the time the last ray of sun disappeared behind the smooth granite walls of the Nest. 
Mavik couldn't get over Emerelda's presence here at the slave camp. The prophecy of Emerelda's arrival had kept him going, not only because he knew that a future awaited him other than the slave camp, but also because of her. He had watched her image in the swirling water countless times before he'd actually met her. And he had dreamed.
That evening, Emerelda and Mavik sat at their own small fire. Opel and the other slaves huddled around a second larger one, a dozen paces to the left. Three Sky Raiders sat at a third blaze, sharing a flask, and playing bone dice. 
The night sky was alight with a thousand stars. A log burnt through its core, the last thread dissolving like a bridge collapsing into a lake of coals. Sparks flew heavenwards, adding golden embers to the silver and indigo sky.
One of the slave women shuffled by and handed Emerelda a pot of balm. "Would you be a dear, and put this on Mavik's whip wounds? I've got to check on Opel's hand." 
Emerelda raised Mavik's robes to his shoulders. He both flushed and froze, standing in just his under clothes. Blood had dried the fabric of his robe to his skin. Her fingertips touched him gently, yet Mavik cringed as pain shot through him. Her hands moved quickly, spreading the ointment into the cracks and crevices that were the violent topography of a slave's back.
"I hate my father. He's a wraithing collaborator." 
 "Hmmm." She pulled the robe back down.
"What does 'hmmm' mean?"
"Hmmm, means I be thinkin'. Don't they be teaching ya nuthin down in yer land of fire? I were just thinkin' that everybody be different. Hard to judge."
Mavik shook his head stubbornly. "I haven't given in."
Emerelda shrugged. "Maybe not, but we all be different."
"Would you become a collaborator?"
"I be hopin' not. I hope I be strong like you, but I ain't none too sure."
Emerelda's tattooed vines moved with her cheekbones as she spoke. A hazel halo encircled her hazel eyes. The fire danced around her and within her.
She's so beautiful.
"The Source is good," Jijari thought. "It has brought you this girl." 
"Don't give me that," Mavik thought, "The Source brought me to this slave camp." 
"The Source only warned you would wind up here. It was man that brought you here, not the divine."
"Then there is no guarantee it will save me. That I'll succeed." 
"The Source does not live our lives for us, but inspires us, pushes us onwards. Grants us the gift of hope."
"Hope is the most cold hearted of all. It's let me down, time and time again."
"You be alright? You looked a bit distracted." Emerelda asked.
"Just lost in thought."
Emerelda took his hand. "Do you be feelin' something towards me?" 
Mavik swallowed. He hoped she could not read some of his baser thoughts, those he even kept from Jijari. Aloud, he said, "Wh-what do you mean?"
Skywolves howled in the distance, two sharp notes followed by a long croon.
Emerelda ran a hand through her uneven hair. "I be feelin' a strange sensation ever since I sawed you, like you be me long lost brother or somethin'."
Brother. His heart sank. I don't want to be her brother.
Here was his chance to broach the topic without appearing to be a lunatic. "Yes. I feel it as well." He hesitated for a moment. "Do you . . . have a second voice inside your mind?" 
Emerelda grabbed Mavik's arm and shook him, her hazel haloes widening in shock. "How ya be knowin'?"
"Easy, now." Mavik grimaced, his wounds brushing against his cloak. "I have one too. Jijari, a water prophetess."  
"You too!" She slapped him on his whip wound and Mavik let out a cry of pain. 
One of the Sky Raider guards barked at them, "Pipe down!"
 She lowered her voice to a whisper. "I thought I be going moon-howlin'! Solita be mine."
Mavik breathed slowly through his nose, trying to keep his heart from splitting. Solita, Kolram's wife. My brother's wife . . .sort of. Had anyone written any guidelines for these types of situations? There were rules about first cousins, but he'd never heard about rules of etiquette for courting the soul host whose second soul was married to your brother's wizard. 
 "I had a twin-brother. He might be dead for all I know. He hosted Grandmaster Kolram."
Emerelda winced and put her hands over her ears. "Alright, Solita! Ouch, enough, for the blood of Bantos!" The Sky Raiders looked in their direction again. Emerelda lowered her voice to a whisper. "Sorry, Solita be jabberin' on: 'her darlin' Kolram' and all that hysterical lovey-dovey stuff. So yer thinkin' yer brother be dead?"
 "I think he's alive, but I'm not sure. When I look for him in the water, sometimes I see a white dragon, but never him."
"Maybe he be turning into a dragon. Stranger things have happened. Having a Guardian witch in me head, fer instance."
"What is Solita like?" Mavik asked.
"Solita be so annoyin', always wantin' me to speak more better. What's wrong with how I be talkin' anyway?"
Mavik smiled. "Nothing. The way you talk is lovely."
"Lovely?" Jijari scoffed. "Men."
Emerelda scoffed as well. "Lovely? Well, I wouldn't be goin' that far. But people understand me decent enough. Anyhow, I don't put much stock in lovely."
"Everything about you is lovely," Mavik thought.
"Abstain from unholy desires!" Jijari said. "The Laws of the Sunken Temple and in your own Red Lands forbid the coveting of your brother's wife."
	"Kolram's wife, not my brother's," Mavik thought. "And it's Emerelda I'm interested in, not Solita. How did everything get so confused?" 
How would Wayden respond if Emerelda and Mavik were to become a couple? Did it even matter? He'd likely never see Wayden again. The thought crushed him. He was angry at Wayden for letting his hand go, but Wayden was still his brother. 
Mavik glanced at Emerelda. Tears streaked her face.
"Sorry. Be thinkin' about home again. Wonderin' how my Da is."
"Jijari foresaw you and me escaping this place. I don't know how, and I don't know when, but I will get you out of here. I promise."
"Don't be makin' any promises ya can't be keepin'." 
She leaned against him.
Mavik was deciding what to say to her next when the sound of gentle snores reached his ears, and he felt her head leaving heavily against him. Their combined warmth helped chase away some of the cold, but still he shivered. 
"I need another fur," he decided. "Maybe someone else will die soon." 

Chapter 9 
Raining Wizards

	The future is not wrought in stone, but flows in water. -Night

-<>-
Wayden

The orphanage gate clanged behind Wayden and Big Darius as they headed onto the street to clear away fallen branches from the previous night's storm. A tall tanglewood tree had been cut right in half by a bolt of lightning and the road was partially blocked.
"Fess up, Toast," Big Darius said, bringing the wheel barrow to a halt. "You used magic on them scagazi, you did."
Wayden threw a stick into the wheel barrow. "You'll never stop asking me about this will you?" 
"Not till you tell the truth." 
The mud squelched under Wayden's shoes. "The truth is you're alive and you should thank the Dragonking for it."
Someone cleared his throat. Wayden had been so engaged in his argument with Big Darius he hadn't even noticed anyone approaching. He turned to see the man he'd seen in the alleyway using soul-stealing magic like a narcotic for the acolyte. The curved dagger still hung on his belt. The cold wind kissed Wayden's face.
Mole-nose smiled. His filigreed robes billowed in the wind and he leaned on a gold-handled cane. "We should all thank the Dragonking for our blessings, shouldn't we?"
Wayden had almost forgotten about Mole-nose. A sudden thought struck Wayden hard in the gut. Could he be standing face to face with the Striker?
Mole-nose sauntered towards them, Viper-Tattoo following silently behind him. Mole-Nose had ornate rings on several fingers and a golden necklace. With so much wealth why had Wayden first met him hanging out in a back alley? 
Mole-nose gestured at Wayden. "You're the boy with the Ozac step-father, aren't you? You want a job? My messenger boy never showed. It'll pay three coppers."
"No thanks," Wayden said.
A gust of wind sent leaves cartwheeling down the cobblestone street.
Big Darius's eyes lit up. "I'll do it!" 
Mole-nose spat out a wad of yellow sour-leaf. "Know how to read, boy?"
Big Darius's face sank. "No."
"Perfect." Mole-nose handed him a sealed parchment. "Take it down to the Coros mansion. You'll get two coins when you get back. Three if you're quick about it."
Wayden grabbed Big Darius by the arm and whispered in his ear. "Don't."
Big Darius shoved Wayden. "Wraith off, Toast. I'll do what I like, witch." 
The sounds of the orphanage gate squeaking open and then slamming shut, made them all turn. Rif was walking towards them, sea-shell braids at full swing. 'Wayden. Mistress Night wants . . ." His eyes rested on Mole Nose. "A word . . ."
The short man noticed Rif staring. "Do I know you?"
"Gior," Rif said. 
"How did you know my name?" Mole-nose narrowed his eyes at Rif. "Who are you?"
"N-n-no one. Nothing. I just heard stories about how great you are. S-s-sorry. I must be-" Rif pushed open the gate and rushed back into the yard. 
Gior stared at him for a moment and then shrugged before sauntering away. Wayden ran to catch up to Rif. "What was that all about?"
Rif was still trembling "Gior studied soul-stealing with Arth, the Dracon, and Dakarth." 
The Dracon and Dakarth-the two leaders that summoned Kolram and the other grandmasters to Darkfist. 
"An alley rat studied with the leaders of Helos and Raslo?"  Wayden asked.
"That alley rat is a d-d-descendant of the Immortal Raylar, like Arth. He was Arth's distant c-c-cousin. Gior inherited some of Raylar's tomes." Rif twisted one of his sea-shell beaded braids. 
"So why would he hang out in an alley?"
"Soul-stealing doesn't work well in a sealed room," Rif said. "Night said you n-n-needed to come quickly."
 "It's not meal time."
Rif shrugged. "She wants her st-st-stew, and to talk to you, right away."
They headed into the main room of the orphanage. 
"Come with me?" Wayden asked.
"She wanted to speak to you alone and Crag told me to clean the sleeping-quarters p-p-post haste."
"Well, the sleeping quarters are on the way. I want to ask you something."
"Alright."
Wayden headed into the scullery, and Cook, looking uncharacteristically subdued, handed him the stew. Cook has heard the rumors that I'm a warlock. He's probably worried I'm going to turn him into a piece of charred toast.
As they ascended the stairs, Wayden asked Rif, "How did Arth learn how to steal souls?"
	"Like Gior, Arth inherited some b-b-books Raylar wrote."
"Kolram told me Raylar experimented with Wraith magic."
	"C-c-correct. He discovered new spells, experimenting on r-r-r-wraiths, scagazi, magic-eaters. Whatever creature he could get hold of. Raylar invented soul-stealing using wraith magic."
They paused on the second floor landing. Wayden glanced around, but no one was nearby. 
"And he taught Dracon Niar, Dakarth, and Gior?" Wayden whispered.
"Yes, but none of them were his equal. Gior was the best of the lot and Dakarth never mastered it at all. They kicked Gior out, claiming he was planning on selling their secrets for money."
"Was it true?"
Rif shrugged. "Probably. Arth never trusted Gior, but he trusted Dakarth and Dracon Niar even less. They wanted to learn soul absorption but wouldn't tell Arth why. Arth gave them the wrong information, teaching them a second method of soul dispersal instead, telling them it was the method for absorption. The souls never went into Asgaroth or the Dracon, they went everywhere else looking for a host."
"A host for what?" Night stood on the staircase. It was the farthest from her room Wayden had ever seen her venture. "Come Wayden, I've something to tell you." Night held the door open for them, smiling like a sweet grandmother. "Rif, my dear, I need to speak to Wayden alone. You can come sing me my lullaby later."
"Good l-l-luck," Rif whispered to Wayden before scurrying away.
Night, dressed in a black gown, studied the water in the basin. For a moment, her eyes pulsed, turning from gray to black. Mavik's eyes had done that too, on the day he painted the water color of the one-eyed bird with the bloody blue beak. The one that predicted Gar Skymaster's coming.
Night's wrinkled hands enveloped Wayden's. "Ah, I'm glad you have come. I had a vision of you in the water, freeing my grandmother. An important juncture."
"Juncture?" Wayden asked.
Night ran a finger through a basin and then extended it upwards. A single drop clung to her bony fingertips, glistening in the candlelight. "The future is not wrought in stone, but flows in water. Junctures are turning points, places where the choices become pivotal."
Wayden placed Cook's bread bowl on a table. Night had claimed her grandmother was the Shadow Queen, and now she said he would free her? "Why would I free the Shadow Queen? She's evil." 
Night waved her hand. "Evil, good. Darkness, light." She lifted up the candle so half her face was shadowed and half illuminated. "Move beyond such simplistic concepts. Is not darkness necessary for light and light for darkness?" Night stepped over to the stew and pulled a chunk of bread from the side of the trencher. "My grandmother and the Weaver allied with the wraiths. The Immortals championed the cause of greed, those who wanted more territory, more power, more wealth, more, more, more. The Shadow Queen wanted them to understand the beauty of the wraiths, that they weren't the enemy."
"All very interesting, but what does this have to do with me?" 
"I'm afraid I cannot say how you'll be used at the end of the day."
That prophecy will never come true, Wayden swore to himself. I'll never free the Immortal who sided with wraiths over the humans.
 Night tore a corner of the bread bowl and held it between gnarled fingers. "The visions are contradictory. I see you in two places on the Night of the Three Moons. And your face isn't burnt in some of the visions. You don't have a twin-brother, do you?" Night cackled at her own joke, but Wayden felt a surge of hope. Could she be seeing Mavik in the water? Was his brother still alive?
Night dabbed the bread in the stew and popped it in her mouth. The food was half-chewed as she said, "Itsh confwusing." Thankfully, she swallowed. "I see you without your burn, freeing my grandmother, and with your burn fighting Gar Skymaster for control of a flaming cat."
"A flaming cat?"
Night stared into the cauldron of water stirring it with her finger. "Interesting."
"What?"
"Are you familiar with the story of Benol and the Crying Bear?"
"I-"
 "Once upon a time, there was a warlock known as the Glass Mage. He was a Beast Tongue and a Glimpser. He controlled an enormous grizzly. Every Flame Day, the bear wreaked havoc in the towns and country, but always with tears in its eyes. The town's folk decided to send a Beast Tongue to the Glass Castle in the clouds. They sent a young Glimpser named Benol."
 "They were both Glimpsers and Beast Tongues just like you, Kolram," Wayden thought.
"Both magic types are forms of mental communication," Kolram replied. "They don't have to go together, but they sometimes do."
"When Benol went up to the castle," Night continued, "he found the warlock sitting on a huge throne made of mirrors in a glass room. The warlock's face was half flesh, half glass. Benol approached the throne, but his feet became reflections."
"How could his feet-?"
"Benol begged the warlock to stop sending the pillaging bear, but the Glass Mage only laughed, saying, 'It is our right as Glimpsers to rule the weak. Join me. We can reign over these lesser beings. Simply give up your warmth and become half glass like me.' Benol refused. The warlock had his grizzly chain Benol in a cell."
Wayden rolled his eyes. "How could a bear chain him?"
Night shrugged. "It must have had hands."
"What type of bear has hands?"
Night whirled around, her eyes darkening. "The type that doesn't keep interrupting!" Night took a breath and straightened her hair. "Benol fought against the warlock, but the Glass Mage was too strong. He gave Benol three days to change his mind. Chains bound his arms and legs, and he hung upside down, naked, in a cold cell. Rats ran up and down his body, but they didn't bother him. In fact, they used their hands to ease his pain."
"How do rats-"
Night silenced Wayden with a glare. "You don't know much about stories, do you?"
"I know they ought to make sense. If you have information, tell it plainly. Don't give me lies about rats with hands."
 "Ah, but there are different types of truths, aren't there?" Night drew close to Wayden. He backed up against the window, afraid of another kiss. "There is the truth that rats have claws, and then there is the deeper truth, that sometimes they have a purpose. Take my grandson for one. Not a whisker on him, but even unwittingly, he plays his role. You mustn't ask how, but instead what: what does it mean? How can I use it? You see what I mean."
"No."
"Excellent," Night spun around, and paced away from Wayden. "Then I'll continue. The grizzly slept outside Benol's cell, but at night the bear wept. Feeling pity for the grizzly, every night Benol sang it to sleep. The Warlock visited Benol for the third and final time, yet still Benol refused to acquiesce. The Glass Mage ordered the grizzly to eat Benol."
"Fine!" Wayden threw up his hands. "The story doesn't have to make sense, but why are you telling me this?"
"Ah, now you are asking the right question," Night lifted the candle, holding it at an angle so wax dripped in a puddle on the table. "Why indeed. Now try to use your ears boy, or whatever you call the cauliflowers on the side of your head. The bear told Benol he would leap from the clouds rather than eat his true friend. Benol told the beast he did not want him to hurt himself. The grizzly said, 'There must be some other way.'  Benol thought for a moment and said, 'If you joined me perhaps we could overpower him together.' Instead of Benol trying to control the Grizzly, like the Glass Mage did, Benol gave his strength to the animal. The bear was free. When the warlock came to see what was taking his pet so long, he found the grizzly unchaining Benol."
"What happened next?" Wayden surprised himself by blurting out the question. 
"The Glass Mage grew mad with rage. He rushed at Benol, who dove aside, and the evil Glimpser broke through the wall of the Glass Castle. To this day, when there is a downpour the people of Glaston say, 'It's raining wizards'."
"That's the end of the story?" Wayden groaned to himself. "That's the great truth that is supposed to help me?"
"Sometimes truths are shy maids," Kolram said. "And don't like to reveal themselves right away." 
Night waved her hand at Wayden. "Now I'm sleepy. I must rest."
Wayden grumbled to himself as he walked back downstairs. He was tired of Water Prophets with their Splasher prophecies. They were too long and said too little. Mavik's watercolors hadn't saved their mother, and Night's stories wouldn't save him either. 
A peal of thunder roared across the sky.
"Great. Now it's raining wizards."
Chapter 10 
With the Shadow of Night

Enjoy today, this moment, this sunset. - Kolram

-<>-  

The next evening, Wayden trudged up the stairs bearing a bowl of rancid-smelling stew. He was so hungry it tempted him, sleep medicine or not. What would Crag do if he found Wayden curled up on the stairwell asleep with an empty bowl of stew?
A burst of lightning illuminated the stairwell. With the shadow of night comes fear, the old saying went. The darkness unnerved him. Goose bumps rose on his arms.
Of course, Rory wouldn't give him a lantern. "Ya ain't in the Telek Manor anymore, Lord Toast," he had said. "You think we got oil to be wastin' on the likes of you? Feel yer way like everyone else."
Rory hadn't minded burning some oil the other night when he dictated yet another of his love poems to the innkeeper's daughter: 'You be beautiful like a mountain. I want to climb you. Let me be your inn and you can keep me.'  Wayden barely managed to keep a straight face during these dictations.
Deprived of both lantern and lunar illumination, making his way was no easy task, especially balancing the tray of stew. Wayden had walked these stairs often enough, but protruding nails and warped wood, made the going precarious even under better circumstances. As he drew closer to the landing, the light from the distant Wall of Flames provided a drop of light.
Wayden could hear Rory and Master Crag arguing.
"We should be tellin' the Fire Guard about what happened in them fields!" Rory shouted. "They be using unauthorized magic." 
 Wayden ground his teeth. He and Rif would have to run for it, if Crag decided to tell the Guard. Luckily, Crag wasn't in agreement. "Oh, go marry yourself a magic-eater, Rory. You've been writing too much poetry and it's made your brain go funny."
"But the law-" Rory said.
"It was the divine power of the Source watching over your sorry arse. No child can master magic." Crag snorted. "Even I haven't mastered Fire-whispering yet and I've been training with an authorized tutor. You'll make us look right fools. No Fire Guard. That's an order."
Wayden paused at the landing, staring out into the gloom. The distant Wall of Flames was a curtain of orange and red. Wayden longed to see a Wall of the World, or for that matter, any wall other than those of the Dracon's Home for Unadoptable Boys. Wayden imagined trekking southwards, upon his sixteenth birthday, to the ice kingdom of Raslo and wreaking vengeance against Gar Skymaster. Perhaps Rif would come as his squire. 
"Wayden, this dream is a child's folly," Kolram said, "Your father led an expedition with a large squadron of armed men to bring Gar to justice. Why would one boy fare better than a small army?"
"I held my own against the scagazi."
"Once again, you either think you are nothing, or you think you can do everything."
"I know, I know." Wayden sighed. "It's just . . . I need to do something. What else can I do?"
"Join the Fire Guard. Convince the Court of Flames to make an offensive. Study magic and become a stronger Beast Tongue than Gar." 
"Those plans would take ages. I've been waiting eight years now. I'm tired of waiting."
"Patience. It will happen in time."
"No, it won't. I'm nothing and nothing will come of me. My father died for nothing. My mother died for nothing. My brother was captured for nothing. And I'll die for nothing too. The Dragonking is a nothing and the Source is a bloody menace."
"Take it a step at a time, young Wayden, for even the smallest of steps matter. Who knows what the future shall hold? There is naught you can do about tomorrow. Enjoy today, this moment, this sunset. The greatest treasures of life belong to orphan and royalty alike."
"It would be easier to enjoy the wraithin' sunset if my stomach wasn't rumbling," Wayden thought.
A creaking sound was racing up the wooden steps below, as the pitter-patter of footfalls approached. Lightning suddenly illuminated the stairwell. Wayden half expected to see Rif or one of the Dariuses heading his way. Instead what he saw was nothing but dancing shadows. Wayden's muscles tightened. 
A shadow bumped into his shoulder as it passed. By some miracle, Wayden held onto the tray. What in the Seven Heavens was that? A shadow with warmth and dimension?
 Wayden's insides quivered. "Was that a ghost?" 
"There are no such things as ghosts," Kolram said. "Well? unless you count me. Though I think I count more as a disembodied spirit-" 
"Then what pushed me?" 
"You imagined it. 'Tis natural for one's nerves to play tricks when we walk alone in the stormy dark." 
Footsteps creaked up the stairs ahead of him, and rusty hinges shrieked as a door opened.
 "That was not my imagination."
Or was it? Shadows did not shove. Some dark force might be murdering the old woman. He had to save her.
 He forced his feet into motion, silently as he could, inching up the stairway. Perhaps it was some assassin or thief skilled in the ways of stealth. But what mortal man could be so stealthy as to be invisible?  Could the intruder be a mage?
"There is no such thing as invisibility magic," Kolram said.
"Wraiths can turn into shadows," Wayden thought.
"The Immortals trapped the wraiths two millennia ago," Kolram said.
"Perhaps they missed one."
Through Night's half-open door, Wayden heard a young girl's voice.
"Tell me about Jazlyn's mother. Did she die in childbirth as the Dracon claimed?" 
A growl of thunder startled Wayden. He barely stopped himself from crying out. The rain drummed against the window glass.
Night said, "You will not believe the truth, Verica."
Verica! The scarred daughter of Healer Berik. What was she doing up here?
The sound of footsteps approached the door. Wayden squeezed behind the doorframe, pressing against the wall and wishing he too could turn invisible. He held his breath, tightness running through him. Why am I so scared of a girl? But then he remembered the dark abyss of the scar. She was no ordinary girl. There was something inside of her. . . A flash of lightning lit up the landing. Wayden swallowed.
The door swung open, brushing against the edge of the tray. Through the edge of the door frame, he saw Verica peering out. She held up a candle and her eyes searched the landing. She shrugged and reentered the room, closing the door behind her. Wayden placed the tray on the floor, and tiptoed closer, pressing his ear against the wood. 
"Thought I heard something," Verica said. "Go on."
"You should not be burdened with such a story my girl," Night said. "A sad tale for tender ears." 
"My ears can take it," Verica said. 
"Did you bring the herbs?"
"Yes."
"Can I have them?"
"After you tell me."
"So be it. Fifteen years ago, the Dracon and Dakarth summoned the Grandmasters to Dark Fist on Three Moons' Night, when magic is at its strongest."
"Why is magic stronger on Three Moons' Night?" Verica asked. 
"How should I know? Do you want to hear the story or not?" Night paused. A glass clinked. "The Draconess, not the cow who calls herself the Draconess now, but the real one. Jazlyn's mother accompanied Dracon Niar. She was with child, and I joined her entourage, in the place of one of her midwives who got the pox. Can I have the herbs?"
"After the story."
 "That evening, I was sent to fetch the water, but got lost. As I passed the temple chambers, I heard screams. I entered, thinking there might be an emergency for which a healer was required."
Wayden moved his foot and the floorboard squeaked. He held his breath.
Night continued, sounding as if in a trance, "On the inner ring stood Flickers clutching bloody knives. The blindfolded corpses of the Grandmasters formed a circle around the entrapped Immortals. I recognized Kolram's corpse. A mist rose from his lips. And from the other Grandmasters as well."
"A witness to our murder!" Kolram said.
 "Mists poured from the lips of all the Grandmasters as blood gushed from their bodies." Night's voice became a whine. "Just a bit now?"
"After the story."
 Exchanging drugs for information wasn't right, but Wayden understood Verica's need to know. His own desire burned like the heart of a dragon. 
"The Dracon chanted, but the Grandmasters' mists drifted away from him. 'What is this? They should be coming to me,' the Dracon had said. But the mists slipped away, drifting out the breached eastern wall of the temple. 'No!' the Dracon cried. Two more mists rose from the Guardian energy shield enshrouding the bodies of Asgaroth and the Dragonking. Asgaroth's had a hint of black to it and the Dragonking's a streak of red.
"Dakarth stared at the black mist, as the Dracon watched the red." Night cleared her throat. "The Dracon held aloft the Sword of Luminescence, chanting, 'Come to me Dragonking, and let your power be mine.' The red mist moved towards the Dracon, who stood with open mouth, as if to receive a worm from a mother bird. The mist swerved, and instead, entered the Sword of Luminscence. The Dracon yelled, 'Arth said the mists would enter me!' The black mist drifted over Asgaroth's body, but the Bone King did not stir from beneath his amber blanket of Guardian magic. Dakarth yelled, 'The mist isn't awakening my father. . . Arth tricked us!'"
"This fits with Rif's account," Kolram said.
"Do you remember any of this?" Wayden asked.
"Well, I was dead at the time."
"No you weren't. You were a mist."
"I don't remember a thing after being blindfolded. That period felt like a bad dream. My first memory after that was of you saying your first word- Crumb Crumb."
Wayden smiled. "I loved that Crumb Crumb."
"He was rather endearing for a stuffed monkey."
Night's voice awoke him from his reveries: "Arth, who was busy lying dead on the stone floor, had very little to say in his own defense. The black mist from Asgaroth floated in my direction. Hoping no one had seen me, I fled down the hallway. The mist followed me. I raced back to the Draconess's chamber and slammed the door shut. I thought I was safe." 
"Asgaroth's spirit was following Night?" Kolram asked, "Toward what end?"
Night continued: "The other midwife screamed at me, 'Where have you been? It's coming. And where is the water?' I didn't know how to explain what had happened. The other midwife sent a serving girl to fetch the water. The Draconess and the other mid-wife screamed and I turned to see the dark mist gathering behind me, pouring through the crack in the door."
Verica gasped.
"The mist flowed past me, hovered over newborn Jazlyn, and then plunged into her crying mouth." Night sniffled. "Some claimed the former Draconess died during the birth, but that was a lie. She arrived safely back from Raslo."
"Jazlyn's mother? She's alive?"
"On that day at least. A few days after we returned, all her regular midwives disappeared. It was because of what we witnessed. I realized they made a mistake and did away with the midwife I replaced in my stead. I shouldn't have told you this. Breathe no word. Even all these years later, if they find out you and I possess such dangerous information, we'll likely disappear ourselves."
 "Another Three Moons' Nights' is approaching next month. Do you think the Dracon will try again?" Verica asked.
"The Dracon has been sending the Fire Guard to imprison magi. That's all the clue you need."
"He's preparing to try again."
"Yes. Your scar. Would I be wrong to suggest you host a wraith?"
"How did you know?"  
 Verica hosts a wraith? Nanny had told him stories about wraiths murdering innocent townsfolk and making clothes of their skin. Cold terror rushed through Wayden's throat. He flung open the door. 
"Oh, wraithin' hell!" Verica said. "I thought you were going to the third floor." The girl was a head shorter than Wayden and stick thin. Not exactly a terrifying sight.
 "Don't move!" Wayden yelled.
 The blackness in Verica's scar swirled and sparkled. Dark material trickled from the crack in her forehead. It spread over her face, like a watercolor transforming itself into a charcoal sketch, until the girl was flesh made shadow. 
Footsteps scampered past him. The door swung open and the tray Wayden had left on the floor went flying, spilling stew across the stairwell. 
"Darius's mercy," Kolram said.
Wayden pursued her. There she was, a part of her at least. Her head and arms had reappeared as well as her legs and feet. Everything in between remained invisible, which made a bizarre sight. Wayden grabbed her hand and pulled her towards him. She fell on top of him. His head swirled and everything turned black.

Chapter 11 
 The Princess and the Mouse

If you were as old as me, you'd come to recognize the folly of mercy and the truth of bones. -Asgaroth

-<>-

The world swirled around him. The Glimpse sucked Wayden deep into Verica's mind. He . . . no she . . . felt so dizzy. It was the poppy Father had given him . . . no, not his father. . . Verica's father . . . Lord Healer Berik . . . Papa . . . had given her. It was so cold. She hurt badly. The leaves dulled the pain, but made her head fuzzy. 
She lay on a table in the lab. Why did Ko force my father to leave in such a rush that he left the lab unlocked? Why did Jazlyn dare me to go in there? The last thing she remembered was reaching for her mumbly ball underneath the table and knocking it over. The vials had shattered - the red and black viscous materials spilled - the burner fell, igniting them. Purple smoke billowed through the room while a shrill noise pierced the air. There was a tiny pop.
 Then the room exploded. She flew into something hard. 
She drifted in and out of consciousness, waking to see Papa standing over her. From this angle his nostrils appeared cavernous. Verica wanted to giggle, but her ribs hurt. Her father's assistant looked nauseous.
A voice moaned from a dark corner of the laboratory. "Release me. I can help."
"I must open the sarcophagus," Papa said.
"Is that wise, My Lord?" the acolyte asked.
"What other choice do I have?" Papa wrung his hands. "She's dying, Source save her." 
Papa started to move away, but the acolyte stopped him. "The Source has nothing to with what's in that sarcophagus."
"Stand aside."
 Papa pushed past his assistant, disappearing from Verica's line of sight. Verica tried calling him back, but her parchment-dry throat refused.
 From the distance, she heard her father ask, "You'll help save my daughter?"
"Only if you free me," the voice moaned.
 "If I do, will you promise to save her?"
 "I promise."
The acolyte muttered, "What good is the promise of a wraith?"
A wraith. Others had whispered rumors that her father worked with wraiths, but she'd always thought them liars. Until now. She shivered.
The sound of metal grinding screeched across the room. Her father reappeared with a man cast in shadows by his side. No, not cast. Composed of shadow. A wraith.
She tried to scream, but it just came out a whimper.
 "Don't worry, Little Mouse." Papa clutched her hand so tightly it hurt. "This is Lukor. He's here to help."
"Wraith . . ." The single word exhausted her.
Lukor's fingers, like shadow knives, raked her skin. Verica's scream froze in her throat.
Lukor's hands settled on her forehead. "There is your entryway. Every mortal has a different one." The dark figure turned towards Papa. "If I do this, it means I'll be bonded with your daughter until the day she dies."
Verica tried again to speak. "Wraiths . . ."
"Are not all bad, Little Mouse," Papa said. "Worry not. Father will take care of you. Lukor, is this the only option?"
"You could let her die," Lukor said. 
A monster? Inside me? She willed herself to flee the lab, but her legs remained stiff as stone.
"You would do this?" Papa asked the wraith.
 "Yes."
"How would it work?"
"We who you call wraiths can cohabit with other souls. Your Immortal, Raylar, learned the secret. He used it to obtain soul mists from people's bodies, but the practice has less aggressive uses. It can be a tool for peaceful co-existence. When I am inside her, my properties will heal her."
"Would you stay with her until she dies a natural death? Do you swear it?"
"I do."
"There's no other way?"
"None. You must decide quickly."
"Do it." Papa's eyes were red and moist. 
Lukor's hands pressed against her forehead, as if jabbing his fingers through it. Her head grew hot, followed by the sensation of a waterfall pouring into her skull. It was bearable, until the burning started. She howled in agony and slammed the back of her head against the table. 
"You're hurting her!" Papa shouted.
 "Peace, Master Berik. As a Healer you know that sometimes pain is necessary to preserve life," Lukor's voice resonated around the room. "I wove her back together. Verica? Can you hear me?" As Lukor spoke his voice switched from outside her to inside. His words sounded warmer now, no longer a cold moan. 
The Glimpse faded. Wayden felt a protruding nail from one of the stairs digging into him. He was sprawled on the staircase, head aching, mouth dry, and limbs numb. 
"Verica . . ." he said, but the shadows surrounding him did not answer.

-<>-
Jazlyn

"Night is sure of this?" Jazlyn paced the scullery, tears racing down her cheeks. 
Verica shrugged. "I told you everything, Jaz. You know as much as I do."
